10079{_}2{_}A MAGAZINE OF LITERATURE, ART, AND POLITICS.
10079{_}3{_}VOL.
10079{_}4{_}II.--JULY, 1858.--NO.
10079{_}5{_}IX.
10079{_}6{_}THE CATACOMBS OF ROME.
10079{_}7{_}[Concluded.]
10079{_}8{_}--fessoque Sacrandum Supponato capiti lapidem, Curistoque quiescam.
10079{_}9{_}PAULINUS OF NOLL  Et factus est in pace locus ejus et halitatio in Sion.
10079{_}10{_}Ps.
10079{_}11{_}LXXV.
10079{_}12{_}2  V.  Rome is preeminently the city of monuments and inscriptions, and the lapidary style is the one most familiar to her.
10079{_}13{_}The Republic, the Empire, the Papacy, the Heathens, and the Christians have written their record upon marble.
10079{_}14{_}But gravestones are proverbially dull reading, and inscriptions are often as cold as the stone upon which they are engraved.
10079{_}15{_}The long gallery of the Vatican, through which one passes to enter the famous library, and which leads to the collection of statues, is lined on one side with heathen inscriptions, of miscellaneous character, on the other with Christian inscriptions, derived chiefly from the catacombs, but arranged with little order.
10079{_}16{_}The comparison thus exhibited to the eye is an impressive one.
10079{_}17{_}The contrast of one class with the other is visible even in external characteristics.
10079{_}18{_}The old Roman lines are cut with precision and evenness; the letters are well formed, the words are rightly spelt, the construction of the sentences is grammatical.
10079{_}19{_}But the Christian inscriptions bear for the most part the marks of ignorance, poverty, and want of skill.
10079{_}20{_}Their lines are uneven, the letters of various sizes, the words ill-spelt, the syntax often incorrect.
10079{_}21{_}Not seldom a mixture of Greek and Latin in the same sentence betrays the corrupt speech of the lower classes, and the Latin itself is that of the common people.
10079{_}22{_}But defects of style and faults of engraving are insufficient to hide the feeling that underlies them.
10079{_}23{_}Besides this great collection of the Vatican, there is another collection now being formed in the _loggia_ of the Lateran Palace, in immediate connection with the Christian Museum.
10079{_}24{_}Arranged as the inscriptions will here be in historic sequence and with careful classification, it will be chiefly to this collection that the student of Christian antiquity will hereafter resort.
10079{_}25{_}It in in the charge of the Cavaliere de Rossi, who is engaged in editing the Christian inscriptions of the first six centuries, and whose extraordinary learning and marvellous sagacity in deciphering and determining the slightest remains of ancient stone-cutting give him unexampled fitness for the work.
10079{_}26{_}Of these inscriptions, about eleven thousand are now known, and of late some forty or fifty have been added each year to the number previously recorded.
10079{_}27{_}But a very small proportion of the eleven thousand remain _in situ_ in the catacombs, and besides the great collections of the Vatican and the Lateran, there are many smaller ones in Rome and in other Italian cities, and many inscriptions originally found in the subterranean cemeteries are now scattered in the porticos or on the pavements of churches in Rome, Ravenna, Milan, and elsewhere.
10079{_}28{_}From the first period of the desecration of the catacombs, the engraved tablets that had closed the graves were almost as much an object of the greed of pious or superstitious marauders as the more immediate relics of the saints.
10079{_}29{_}Hence came their dispersion through Italy, and hence, too, it has happened that many very important and interesting inscriptions belonging to Rome are now found scattered through the Continent.
10079{_}30{_}It has been, indeed, sometimes the custom of the Roman Church to enhance the value of a gift of relics by adding to it the gift of the inscription on the grave from which they were taken.
10079{_}31{_}A curious instance of this kind, connected with the making of a very popular saint, occurred not many years since.
10079{_}32{_}In the year 1802 a grave was found in the Cemetery of St. Priscilla, by which were the remains of a glass vase that had held blood, the indication of the burial-place of a martyr.
10079{_}33{_}The grave was closed by three tiles, on which were the following words painted in red letters: LVMENA PAXTE CVMFL.
10079{_}34{_}There were also rudely painted on the tiles two anchors, three darts, a torch, and a palm-branch.
10079{_}35{_}The bones found within the grave, together with the tiles bearing the inscription, were placed in the Treasury of Relics at the Lateran.
10079{_}36{_}On the return of Pius VII., one of the deputation of Neapolitan clergy sent to congratulate him sought and received from the Pope these relics and the tiles as a gift for his church.
10079{_}37{_}The inscription had been read by placing the first tile after the two others, thus,--PAX TECUM FILUMENA, _Peace be with thee, Filumena_; and Filumena was adopted as a new saint in the long list of those to whom the Roman Church has given this title.
10079{_}38{_}It was supposed, that, in the haste of closing the grave, the tiles had been thus misplaced.
10079{_}39{_}Very soon after the gift, a priest, who desired not to be named _on account of his great humility_, had a vision at noonday, in which the beautiful virgin with the beautiful name appeared to him and revealed to him that she had suffered death rather than yield her chastity to the will of the Emperor, who desired to make her his wife.
10079{_}40{_}Thereupon a young artist, whose name is also suppressed, likewise had a vision of St. Filomena, who told him that the emperor was Diocletian; but as history stands somewhat opposed to this statement, it has been suggested that the artist mistook the name, and that the Saint said Maximian.
10079{_}41{_}However this may be, the day of her martyrdom was fixed on the 10th of August, 303.
10079{_}42{_}Her relics were carried to Naples with great reverence; they were inclosed, after the Neapolitan fashion, in a wooden doll of the size of life, dressed in a white satin skirt and a red tunic, with a garland of flowers on its head, and a lily and a dart in its hand.
10079{_}43{_}This doll, with the red- lettered tiles, was soon transferred to its place in the church of Mugnano, a small town not far from Naples.
10079{_}44{_}Many miracles were wrought on the way, and many have since been wrought in the church itself.
10079{_}45{_}The fame of the virgin spread through Italy, and chapels were dedicated to her honor in many distant churches; from Italy it reached Germany and France, and it has even crossed the Atlantic to America.
10079{_}46{_}Thus a new saint, a new story, and a new exhibition of credulity had their rise not long ago from a grave and three words in the catacombs.
10079{_}47{_}One of the first differences which are obvious, in comparing the Christian with the heathen mortuary inscriptions, is the introduction in the former of some new words, expressive of the new ideas that prevailed among them.
10079{_}48{_}Thus, in place of the old formula which had been in most common use upon gravestones, D.M., or, in Greek, [Greek: TH.K.
10079{_}49{_}], standing for _Dis Manibus_, or [Greek: _Theois karachthoniois_], a dedication of the stone to the gods of death, we find constantly the words _In pace_.
10079{_}50{_}The exact meaning of these words varies on different inscriptions, but their general significance is simple and clear.
10079{_}51{_}When standing alone, they seem to mean that the dead rests in the peace of God; sometimes they are preceded by _Requiescat_, "May he rest in peace"; sometimes there is the affirmation, _Dormit in pace_, "He sleeps in peace"; sometimes a person is said _recessisse in pace_, "to have departed in peace."
10079{_}52{_}Still other forms are found, as, for instance, _Vivas in pace_, "Live in peace," or _Suscipiatur in pace_, "May he be received into peace,"--all being only variations of the expression of the Psalmist's trust, "I will lay me down in peace and sleep, for thou, Lord, only makest me dwell in safety."
10079{_}53{_}It is a curious fact, however, that on some of the Christian tablets the same letters which were used by the heathens have been found.
10079{_}54{_}One inscription exists beginning with the words _Dis Manibus_, and ending with the words _in pace_.
10079{_}55{_}But there is no need of finding a difficulty in this fact, or of seeking far for an explanation of it.
10079{_}56{_}As we have before remarked, in speaking of works of Art, the presence of some heathen imagery and ideas in the multitude of the paintings and inscriptions in the catacombs is not so strange as the comparatively entire absence of them.
10079{_}57{_}Many professing Christians must have had during the early ages but an imperfect conception of the truth, and can have separated themselves only partially from their previous opinions, and from the conceptions that prevailed around them in the world.
10079{_}58{_}To some the letters of the heathen gravestones, and the words which they stood for, probably appeared little more than a form expressive of the fact of death, and, with the imperfect understanding natural to uneducated minds, they used them with little thought of their absolute significance.
10079{_}59{_}[1]  [Footnote 1: It is probable that most of the gravestones upon which this heathen formula is found are not of an earlier date than the middle of the fourth century.
10079{_}60{_}At this time Christianity became the formal religion of many who were still heathen in character and thought, and cared little about the expression of a faith which they had adopted more from the influence of external motives than from principle or conviction.]
10079{_}61{_}Another difference in words which is very noticeable, running through the inscriptions, is that of _depositus_, used by the Christians to signify the _laying away_ in the grave, in place of the heathen words _situs, positus, sepultus, conditus_.
10079{_}62{_}The very name of _coemeterium_, adopted by the Christians for their burial-places, a name unknown to the ancient Romans, bore a reference to the great doctrine of the Resurrection.
10079{_}63{_}Their burial-ground was a _cemetery_, that is, a _sleeping-place_; they regarded the dead as put there to await the awakening; the body was _depositus_, that is, _intrusted to_ the grave, while the heathen was _situs_ or _sepultus, interred_ or _buried_,--the words implying a final and definitive position.
10079{_}64{_}And as the Christian _dormit_ or _quiescit, sleeps_ or _rests_ in death, so the heathen is described as _abreptus_, or _defunctus, snatched away_ or _departed_ from life.
10079{_}65{_}Again, the contrast between the inscriptions is marked, and in a sadder way, by the difference of the expressions of mourning and grief.
10079{_}66{_}No one who has read many of the ancient gravestones but remembers the bitter words that are often found on them,--words of indignation against the gods, of weariness of life, of despair and unconsoled melancholy.
10079{_}67{_}Here is one out of many:--    PROCOPE MANVS LEBO CONTRA   DEVM QVI ME INNOCENTEM SVS   TVLIT QVAE VIXI ANNOS XX.
10079{_}68{_}POS.
10079{_}69{_}PROCLVS.
10079{_}70{_}I, Procope, who lived twenty years, lift up   my hands against God, who took me away innocent.
10079{_}71{_}Proclus set up this.
10079{_}72{_}But among the Christian inscriptions of the first centuries there is not one of this sort.
10079{_}73{_}Most of them contain no reference to grief; they are the very short and simple words of love, remembrance, and faith,--as in the following from the Lateran:--    ADEODATE DIGNAE ET MERITAE VIRGINI   ETQVIESCE HIC IN PACE IVBENTE XPO EJUS    To Adeodata, a worthy and deserving Virgin,   and rests here in peace, her Christ commanding.
10079{_}74{_}On a few the word _dolens_ is found, simply telling of grief.
10079{_}75{_}On one to the memory of a sweetest daughter the word _irreparable_ is used, _Filiae dulcissimae inreparabili_.
10079{_}76{_}Another is, "To Dalmatius, sweetest son, whom his _unhappy_ father was not permitted to enjoy for even seven years."
10079{_}77{_}Another inscription, in which something of the feeling that was unchecked among the heathens finds expression in Christian words, is this: "Sweet soul.
10079{_}78{_}To the incomparable child, who lived seventeen years, and _undeserving_ [of death] gave up life in the peace of the Lord."
10079{_}79{_}Neither the name of the child nor of the parents is on the stone, and the word _immeritus_, which is used here, and which is common in heathen use, is found, we believe, on only one other Christian grave.
10079{_}80{_}One inscription, which has been interpreted as being an expression of unresigned sorrow, is open to a very different signification.
10079{_}81{_}It is this:--    INNOCENTISSISSIMAE ETATIS   DVLCISSIMO FILIO   JOVIANO QVI VIXIT ANN.
10079{_}82{_}VII   ET MENSES VI NON MERENTES   THEOCTISTVS ET THALLVSA PARENTES    To their sweetest boy Jovian, of the most   innocent age, who lived seven years and six   months, his undeserving [or unlamenting] parents   Theoctistus and Thallusa.
10079{_}83{_}Here, without forcing the meaning, _non merentes_ might be supposed to refer to the parents' not esteeming themselves worthy  to be left in possession of such a treasure; but the probability is that _merentes_ is only a misspelling of _maerentes_ for otherwise _immerentes_ would have been the natural word.
10079{_}84{_}But it is thus that the Christian inscriptions must be sifted, to find expressions at variance with their usual tenor, their general composure and trust.
10079{_}85{_}The simplicity and brevity of the greater number of them are, indeed, striking evidence of the condition of feeling among those who set them upon the graves.
10079{_}86{_}Their recollections of the dead feared no fading, and Christ, whose coming was so near at hand, would know and reunite his own.
10079{_}87{_}Continually we read only a name with _in pace_, without date, age, or title, but often with some symbol of love or faith hastily carved or painted on the stone or tiles.
10079{_}88{_}Such inscriptions as the following are common:--    FELICISSIMVS DVLCIS,--GAVDENTIA IN PACE,   --SEVERA IN DEO VIVAS,--  or, with a little more fulness of expression,--    DVLCISSIMO FILIO ENDELECIO   BENEMERENTI QVI VIXIT   ANNOS II MENSE VNV   DIES XX IN PACE    To the sweetest son Endelechius, the well-   deserving, who lived two years, one month,   twenty days.
10079{_}89{_}In peace.
10079{_}90{_}The word _benemerenti_ is of constant recurrence.
10079{_}91{_}It is used both of the young and the old; and it seems to have been employed, with comprehensive meaning, as an expression of affectionate and grateful remembrance.
10079{_}92{_}Here is another short and beautiful epitaph.
10079{_}93{_}The two words with which it begins are often found.
10079{_}94{_}ANIMA DVLCIS AVFENIA VIRGO   BENEDICTA QVE VIXIT ANN: XXX   DORMIT IN PACE    Sweet Soul.
10079{_}95{_}The Blessed Virgin Aufenia,   who lived thirty years.
10079{_}96{_}She sleeps in peace.
10079{_}97{_}But the force and tenderness of such epitaphs as these is hardly to be recognized in single examples.
10079{_}98{_}There is a cumulative pathos in them, as one reads, one after another, such as these that follow:--    ANGELICE BENE IN PACE    To Angelica well in peace.
10079{_}99{_}CVRRENTIO SERVO DEI DEP.
10079{_}100{_}D. XVI.
10079{_}101{_}KAL   NOVEM.
10079{_}102{_}To Currentius, the servant of God, laid in   the grave on the sixteenth of the Kalends of   November.
10079{_}103{_}MAXIMINVS QVI VIXIT ANNOS XXIII   AMICVS OMNIVM    Maximin, who lived twenty-three years, the   friend of all.
10079{_}104{_}SEPTIMVS MARCIANE   IN PACE QUE BICSIT MECV   ANNOS XVII.
10079{_}105{_}DORMIT IN PACE    Septimus to Marciana in peace.
10079{_}106{_}Who lived   with me seventeen years.
10079{_}107{_}She sleeps in peace.
10079{_}108{_}GAVDENTIA   PAVSAT DVLCIS   SPIRITVS ANNORVM II   MENSORVM TRES.
10079{_}109{_}Gaudentia rests.
10079{_}110{_}Sweet spirit of two years   and three months.
10079{_}111{_}Here is a gravestone with the single word VIATOR; here one that tells only that Mary placed it for her daughter; here one that tells of the light of the house,--[Greek: To phos thaes Oikias].
10079{_}112{_}Nor is it only in these domestic and intimate inscriptions that the habitual temper and feeling of the Christians is shown, but even still more in those that were placed over the graves of such members of the household of faith as had made public profession of their belief, and shared in the sufferings of their Lord.
10079{_}113{_}There is no parade of words on the gravestones of the martyrs.
10079{_}114{_}Their death needed no other record than the little jar of blood placed in the mortar, and the fewest words were enough where this was present.
10079{_}115{_}Here is an inscription in the rudest letters from a martyr's grave:--    SABATIVS BENEMERENTI QVI VIXIT ANNOS XL    To the well-deserving Sabatias, who lived   forty years.
10079{_}116{_}And here another:--    PROSPERO INNOCENTI ANIMAE IN PACE.
10079{_}117{_}To Prosperus, innocent soul, in peace.
10079{_}118{_}And here a third, to a child who had died as one of the Innocents:--    MIRAE INNOCENTIAE ANIMA DULCIS AEMILEANVS   QVI VIXIT ANNO VNO, MENS.
10079{_}119{_}VIII D. XXVIII   DORMIT IN PACE    Aemilian, sweet soul of marvellous innocence,   who lived one year, eight months, twenty-eight   days.
10079{_}120{_}He sleeps in peace.
10079{_}121{_}At this grave was found the vase of blood, and on the gravestone was the figure of a dove.
10079{_}122{_}Another inscription, which preserves the name of one of those who suffered in the most severe persecution to which the ancient Church was exposed, and which, if genuine, is, so far as known, the only monument of the kind, is marked by the same simplicity of style:--    LANNVS XPI MA   RTIR HC*[Hic?]
10079{_}123{_}REQVIESC   IT SVR [E-P-S] DIOCLITI ANO PASSVS    Lannus Martyr of Christ here rests.
10079{_}124{_}He   suffered under Diocletian.
10079{_}125{_}The three letters EPS have been interpreted as standing for the words _et posteris suis_, and as meaning that the grave was also for his successors.
10079{_}126{_}Not yet, then, had future saints begun to sanctify their graves, and to claim the exclusive possession of them.
10079{_}127{_}But there is another point of contrast between the inscriptions of the un- Christianized and the Christian Romans, which illustrates forcibly the difference in the regard which they paid to the dead.
10079{_}128{_}To the one the dead were still of this world, and the greatness of life, the distinctions of class, the titles of honor still clung to them; to the other the past life was as nothing to that which had now begun.
10079{_}129{_}The heathen epitaphs are loaded with titles of honor, and with the names of the offices which the dead had borne, and, like the modern Christian (?)
10079{_}130{_}epitaphs whose style has been borrowed from them, the vanity of this world holds its place above the grave.
10079{_}131{_}But among the early Christian inscriptions of Rome nothing of this kind is known.
10079{_}132{_}Scarcely a title of rank or a name of office is to be found among them.
10079{_}133{_}A military title, or the name of priest or deacon, or of some other officer in the Church, now and then is met with; but even these, for the most part, would seem to belong to the fourth century, and never contain any expression of boastfulness or flattery.
10079{_}134{_}FL.
10079{_}135{_}OLIVS PATERNVS   CENTVRIO CHOR.
10079{_}136{_}X VRB.
10079{_}137{_}QVI VIXIT AH XXVII   IN PACE    Flavius Olius Paternus, Centurion of the   Tenth Urban Cohort, who lived twenty-seven   years.
10079{_}138{_}In peace.
10079{_}139{_}It is true, no doubt, that among the first Christians there were very few of the rich and great.
10079{_}140{_}The words of St. Paul to the Corinthians were as true of the Romans as of those to whom they were specially addressed: "For ye see your calling, brethren, how that not many wise men after the flesh, not many mighty, not many noble are called."
10079{_}141{_}Still there is evidence enough that even in the first two centuries some of the mighty and some of the noble at Rome were among those called, but that evidence is not to be gathered from the gravestones of the catacombs.
10079{_}142{_}We have seen, in a former article, that even the grave of one of the early bishops,--the highest officer of the Church,--and one who had borne witness to the truth in his death, was marked by the words,    CORNELIVS MARTYR   EP.
10079{_}143{_}The Martyr Cornelius, Bishop.
10079{_}144{_}Compare this with the epitaphs of the later popes, as they are found on their monuments in St. Peter's,--"flattering, false insculptions on a tomb, and in men's hearts reproach,"--epitaphs overweighted with superlatives, ridiculous, were it not for their impiety, and full of the lies and vanities of man in the very house of God.
10079{_}145{_}With this absence of boastfulness and of titles of rank on the early Christian graves two other characteristics of the inscriptions are closely connected, which bear even yet more intimate and expressive relation to the change wrought by Christianity in the very centre of the heathen world.
10079{_}146{_}"One cannot study a dozen monuments of pagan Rome," says Mr. Northcote, in his little volume on the catacombs, "without reading something of _servus_ or _libertus, libertis libertabusque posterisque eorum_; and I believe the proportion in which they are found is about three out of every four.
10079{_}147{_}Yet, in a number of Christian inscriptions exceeding eleven thousand, and all belonging to the first six centuries of our era, scarcely six have been found containing any allusion whatever--and even two or three of these are doubtful--to this fundamental division of ancient Roman society.
10079{_}148{_}"No one, we think, will be rash enough to maintain, either that this omission is the result of mere accident, or that no individual slave or freedman was ever buried in the catacombs.
10079{_}149{_}Rather, these two cognate facts, the absence from ancient Christian epitaphs of all titles of rank and honor on the one hand, or of disgrace and servitude on the other, can only be adequately explained by an appeal to the religion of those who made them.
10079{_}150{_}The children of the primitive Church did not record upon their monuments titles of earthly dignity, because they knew that with the God whom they served 'there was no respect of persons'; neither did they care to mention the fact of their bondage, or of their deliverance from bondage, to some earthly master, because they thought only of that higher and more perfect liberty wherewith Christ had set them free; remembering that 'he that was called, being a bondman, was yet the freeman of the Lord, and likewise he that was called, being free, was still the bondman of Christ.'
10079{_}151{_}"And this conclusion is still further confirmed by another remarkable fact which should be mentioned, namely, that there are not wanting in the catacombs numerous examples of another class of persons, sometimes ranked among slaves, but the mention of whose servitude, such as it was, served rather to record an act of Christian charity than any social degradation; I allude to the alumni, or foundlings, as they may be called.
10079{_}152{_}The laws of pagan Rome assigned these victims of their parents' crimes or poverty to be the absolute property of any one who would take charge of them.
10079{_}153{_}As nothing, however, but compassion could move a man to do this, children thus acquired were not called _servi_, as though they were slaves who had been bought with money, nor _vernae_, as though they had been the children of slaves born in the house, but _alumni_, a name simply implying that they had been brought up (_ab alendo_) by their owners.
10079{_}154{_}Now it is a very singular fact, that there are actually more instances of _alumni_ among the sepulchral inscriptions of Christians than among the infinitely more numerous inscriptions of pagans, showing clearly that this was an act of charity to which the early Christians were much addicted; and the _alumni_, when their foster-parents died, very properly and naturally recorded upon their tombs this act of charity, to which they were themselves so deeply indebted."
10079{_}155{_}So far Mr. Northcote.
10079{_}156{_}It is still further to be noted, as an expression of the Christian temper, as displayed in this kind of charity, that it never appears in the inscriptions as furnishing a claim for praise, or as being regarded as a peculiar merit.
10079{_}157{_}There is no departure from the usual simplicity of the gravestones in those of this class.
10079{_}158{_}[Greek:   PETROS   THREPTOS   RAUKUTA   TOS EN THEO]    Peter, sweetest foster-child, in God.
10079{_}159{_}And a dove is engraved at either side of this short epitaph.
10079{_}160{_}VITALIANO ALVMNO KARO   EVTROPIVS FECIT.
10079{_}161{_}Eutropius made this for the dear foster-child   Vitalian.
10079{_}162{_}ANTONIVS DISCOLIVS FILIVS ET BIBIVS   FELLICISSIMVS ALVMNVS VALERIE CRESTENI   MATRI BIDVE ANORVM XVIII INTET SANCTOS    Antonius Discolius her son, and Bibius Felicissimus   her foster-child, to Valeria Crestina   their mother, a widow for eighteen years.
10079{_}163{_}[Her grave is] among the holy.
10079{_}164{_}[2]  [Footnote 2: This inscription is not of earlier date than the fourth century, as is shown by the words, _Inter sancios_,--referring, as we heretofore stated, to the grave being made near that of some person esteemed a saint.]
10079{_}165{_}These inscriptions lead us by a natural transition to such as contain some reference to the habits of life or to the domestic occupations and feelings of the early Christians.
10079{_}166{_}Unfortunately for the gratification of the desire to learn of these things, this class of inscriptions is far from numerous,--and the common conciseness is rarely, in the first centuries, amplified by details.
10079{_}167{_}But here is one that tells a little story in itself:--                 DOMNINAE INNOCENTISSINAE ET DVLCISSIMAE COIVGI   QVAE VIXIT ANN XVI M. IIII ET FVIT  IMARITATA ANN.
10079{_}168{_}DVOBVS M. IIII D. VIIII   CVM QVA SON LICVIT FVISSE PROPTER        CAVSAS PEREGRINATIONIS            NISI MENEIE VI QVO TEMPORE VT EGO SENSI ET EXHBVI             AMOREM MEVM    NVLLI SV ALII SIC DILEXERVNT        DEPOSIT XV KAL.
10079{_}169{_}IVN.
10079{_}170{_}To Domnina, my most innocent and sweetest   wife; who lived sixteen years and four   months, and was married two years, four   months, and nine days; with whom, on account   of my journeys, I was permitted to be   only six months; in which time, as I felt, so   I showed my love.
10079{_}171{_}No others have so loved   one another.
10079{_}172{_}Placed in the grave the 15th   of the Kalends of June.
10079{_}173{_}Who was this husband whose far-off journeys had so separated him from his lately married wife?
10079{_}174{_}Who were they who so loved as no others had loved?
10079{_}175{_}The tombstone gives only the name of Domnina.
10079{_}176{_}But in naming her, and in the expression of her husband's love, it gives evidence, which is confirmed by many other tokens in the catacombs, of the change introduced by Christianity in the position of women, and in the regard paid to them.
10079{_}177{_}Marriage was invested with a sanctity which redeemed it from sensuality, and Christianity became the means of uniting man and woman in the bonds of an immortal love.
10079{_}178{_}Here is an inscription which, spite of the rudeness of its style, preserves the pleasant memory of a Roman child:--    ISPIRITO SANTO BONO   FLORENTIO QVI VIXIT ANIS XIII   QVAM SI FILIVM SVVM ET COTDEVS   MATER FILIO BENEMERETI FECERVNT.
10079{_}179{_}To the good and holy spirit Florentius, who   lived thirteen years, Coritus, his master, who   loved him more than if he were his own son,   and Cotdeus, his mother, have made this for   her well-deserving son.
10079{_}180{_}[3]  [Footnote 3: Compare an inscription from a heathen tomb:--    C. JVLIVS MAXIMVS   ANN.
10079{_}181{_}II.
10079{_}182{_}M. V.    ATROX O FORTVNA TRVCI QVAE FVNERR GAVDES   QVID MIHI TAM SVBITO MAXIMVS ERIPITVR   QVI MODO JVCVNDVS GREMIO SVPERESSE SOLEBAT   HIC LAPIS TN TVMVLO NVNC JACET ECCE MATER    C. Julius Maximus,   Two years, five months old.
10079{_}183{_}Harsh Fortune, that in cruel death finds't joy,   Why is my Maximus thus sudden reft,   So late the pleasant burden of my breast?
10079{_}184{_}Now in the grave this stone lies: lo, his mother!]
10079{_}185{_}And Coritus, his master, and Cotdeus, his mother, might have rejoiced in knowing that their poor, rough tablet would keep the memory of her boy alive for so many centuries; and that long after they had gone to the grave, the good spirit of Florentius should still, through these few words, remain to work good upon the earth.--Note in this inscription (as in many others) the Italianizing of the old Latin,--the _ispirito_, and the _santo_; note also the mother's strange name, reminding one of Puritan appellations,--Cotdeus being the abbreviation of _Quod vult Deus_, "What God wills.
10079{_}186{_}"[4]  [Footnote 4: Other names of this kind were _Deogratias_, _Habetdeum_, and _Adeodatus_.]
10079{_}187{_}Here is an inscription set up by a husband to his wife, Dignitas, who was a woman of great goodness and entire purity of life:--    QUE SINE LESIONE ANIMI MEI VIXI MECVM   ANNOS XV FILIOS AVTEM PROCREAVIT VII   EX QVIBVS SECV ABET AD DOMINVM IIII    Who, without ever wounding my soul, lived   with me for fifteen years, and bore seven   children, four of whom she has with her in   the Lord.
10079{_}188{_}We have already referred to the inscriptions which bear the name of some officer of the early Church; but there is still another class, which exhibits in clear letters others of the designations and customs familiar to the first Christians.
10079{_}189{_}Thus, those who had not yet been baptized and received into the fold, but were being instructed in Christian doctrine for that end, were called _catechumens_; those who were recently baptized were called _neophytes_; and baptism itself appears sometimes to have been designated by the word _illuminatio_.
10079{_}190{_}Of the use of these names the inscriptions give not infrequent examples.
10079{_}191{_}It was the custom also among the Christians to afford support to the poor and to the widows of their body.
10079{_}192{_}Thus we read such inscriptions as the following:--    RIGINE VENEMEREMTI FILIA SVA FECIT   VENERIGINE MATRI VIDVAE QVE SE   DIT VIDVA ANNOS LX ET ECLESA   VIXIT ANNOS LXXX MESIS V   DIES XXVI    Her daughter Reneregina made this for her   well-deserving mother Regina, a widow, who   sat a widow sixty years, and never burdened   the church, the wife of one husband, who lived   eighty years, five months, twenty-six days.
10079{_}193{_}The words of this inscription recall to mind those of St. Paul, in his First Epistle to Timothy, (v. 3-16,) and especially the verse, "If any man or woman that believeth have widows, let them relieve them, and let not the church be charged."
10079{_}194{_}Some of the inscriptions preserve a record of the occupation or trade of the dead, sometimes in words, more often by the representation of the implements of labor.
10079{_}195{_}Here, for instance, is one which seems like the advertisement of a surviving partner:--    DE BIANOBA   POLLECLA QVE ORDEV BENDET DE   BIANOBA    From New Street.
10079{_}196{_}Pollecla, who sold barley   on New Street.
10079{_}197{_}Others often bear a figure which refers to the name of the deceased, an _armoirie parlante_ as it were, which might be read by those too ignorant to read the letters on the stone.
10079{_}198{_}Thus, a lion is scratched on the grave of a man named Leo; a little pig on the grave of the little child Porcella, who had lived not quite four years; on the tomb of Dracontius is a dragon; and by the side of the following charming inscription is found the figure of a ship:--    NABIRA IN PACE ANIMA DULCIS   QVI BIXIT ANOS XVI M V   ANIMA MELEIEA   TITVLV FACTV   APARENTES SIGNVM NABE    Navira in peace.
10079{_}199{_}Sweet soul, who lived sixteen   years, five months.
10079{_}200{_}Soul honey-sweet.
10079{_}201{_}This inscription made by her parents.
10079{_}202{_}The   sign a ship.
10079{_}203{_}The figures that are most frequent upon the sepulchral slabs are, however, not such as bear relation to a name or profession, but the commonly adopted symbols of the faith, similar in design and character to those exhibited in the paintings of the catacombs.
10079{_}204{_}The Good Shepherd is thus often rudely represented; the figure of Jonah is naturally, from its reference to the Resurrection, also frequently found; and the figure of a man or woman with arms outstretched, in the attitude of prayer, occurs on many of the sepulchral slabs.
10079{_}205{_}The anchor, the palm, the crown, and the dove, as being simpler in character and more easily represented, are still more frequently found.
10079{_}206{_}The varying use of symbols at different periods has been one of the means which have assisted in determining approximate dates for the inscriptions upon which they are met with.
10079{_}207{_}It is a matter of importance, in many instances, to fix a date to an inscription.
10079{_}208{_}Historical and theological controversies hang on such trifles.
10079{_}209{_}Most of the early gravestones bear no date; and it was not till the fourth century, that, with many other changes, the custom of carving a date upon them became general.
10079{_}210{_}The century to which an inscription belongs may generally be determined with some confidence, either by the style of expression and the nature of the language, or by the engraved character, or some other external indications.
10079{_}211{_}Among these latter are the symbols.
10079{_}212{_}It has, for instance, been recently satisfactorily proved by the Cavaliere de Rossi that the use of the emblem of the fish in the catacombs extended only to the fourth century, so that the monuments upon which it is found may, with scarcely an exception, be referred to the preceding period.
10079{_}213{_}As this emblem went out of use, owing perhaps to the fact that the Christians were no longer forced to seek concealment for their name and profession, the famous monogram of Christ, [Symbol] the hieroglyphic, not only of his name, but of his cross, succeeded to it, and came, indeed, into far more general use than that which the fish had ever attained.
10079{_}214{_}The monogram is hardly to be found before the time of Constantine, and, as it is very frequently met with in the inscriptions from the catacombs, it affords an easy means, in the absence of a more specific date, for determining a period earlier than which any special inscription bearing it cannot have originated.
10079{_}215{_}Its use spread rapidly during the fourth century.
10079{_}216{_}It "became," says Gibbon, with one of his amusing sneers, "extremely fashionable in the Christian world."
10079{_}217{_}The story of the vision of Constantine was connected with it, and the Labarum displayed its form in the front of the imperial army.
10079{_}218{_}It was thus not merely the emblem of Christ, but that also of the conversion of the Emperor and of the fatal victory of the Church.
10079{_}219{_}It is a remarkable fact, and one which none of the recent Romanist authorities attempt to controvert, that the undoubted earlier inscriptions afford no evidence of any of the peculiar doctrines of the Roman Church.
10079{_}220{_}There is no reference to the doctrine of the Trinity to be found among them; nothing is to be derived from them in support of the worship of the Virgin; her name even is not met with on any monument of the first three centuries; and none of the inscriptions of this period give any sign of the prevalence of the worship of saints.
10079{_}221{_}There is no support of the claim of the Roman Church to supremacy, and no reference to the claim of the Popes to be the Vicars of Christ.
10079{_}222{_}As the third century advances to its close, we find the simple and crude beginning of that change in Christian faith which developed afterward into the broad idea of the intercessory power of the saints.
10079{_}223{_}Among the earlier inscriptions prayers to God or to Christ are sometimes met with, generally in short exclamatory expressions concerning the dead.
10079{_}224{_}Thus we find at first such words as these:--    AMERIMNVS   RVFINAE COIV   GI CARISSIME   BENEMEREN   TI SPIRITVM   TVVM DEVS   REFRIGERET    Amerimnus to his dearest wife Rufina well-   deserving.
10079{_}225{_}May God refresh thy spirit!
10079{_}226{_}And, in still further development,--    [Greek: AUR.
10079{_}227{_}AIANOS PAPHLAGON THEOU   DOULOS PISTOS   EKOIMNON EN EIPNIN MINSON   AUTOU   O THEOS EIS TOUS AIONAS]    Aurelius Aelianus, a Paphlagonian, faithful   servant of God.
10079{_}228{_}He sleeps in peace.
10079{_}229{_}Remember   him, O God, forever!
10079{_}230{_}Again, two sons ask for their mother,--    DOMINE NE QVANDO   ADVMBRETVR SPIRITVS   VENERES    O Lord, let not the spirit of Venus be shadowed   at any time!
10079{_}231{_}From such petitions as these we come by a natural transition to such as are addressed to the dead themselves, as being members of the same communion with the living, and uniting in prayers with those they had left on earth and for their sake.
10079{_}232{_}VIBAS IN PACE ET PETE PRO NOBIS    Mayst thou live in peace and ask for us!
10079{_}233{_}Or, as in another instance,--    PETE PRO PARENTES TVOS   MATRONATA MATRONA   QVE VIXIT AN.
10079{_}234{_}I.
10079{_}235{_}DI.
10079{_}236{_}LII.
10079{_}237{_}Pray for thy parents, Matronata Matrona!
10079{_}238{_}Who lived one year, fifty-two days.
10079{_}239{_}And as we have seen how in the fourth century the desire arose of being buried near the graves of those reputed holy, so by a similar process we find this simple and affectionate petition to the dead passing into a prayer for the dead to those under whose protection it was hoped that they might be.
10079{_}240{_}In the multitude of epitaphs, however, these form but a small number.
10079{_}241{_}Here is one that begins with a heathen formula:--    SOMNO HETERNALI   AVRELIVS GEMELLVS QVI BIXIT AN--   ET MESES VIII DIES XVIII MATER FILIO   CARISSIMO BENAEMERENTI FECIT IN PA--   [C]ONMANDO BASSILA INNOCENTIA GEMELLI    In Eternal Sleep.
10079{_}242{_}Aurelius Gemellus, who   lived --- years, and eight months, eighteen   days.
10079{_}243{_}His mother made this for her dearest   well-deserving son in peace.
10079{_}244{_}I commend to   Basilla the innocence of Gemellus.
10079{_}245{_}Basilla was one of the famous martyrs of the time of Valerian and Gallienus.
10079{_}246{_}Here again is another inscription of a curious character, as interposing a saint between the dead and his Saviour.
10079{_}247{_}The monogram marks its date.
10079{_}248{_}RVTA OMNIBVS SVBDITA ET ATFABI   LIS BIBET IN NOMINE PETRI   IN PACE    Ruta, subject and affable to all, shall live in   the name of Peter, in the peace of Christ.
10079{_}249{_}But it would seem from other inscriptions as if the new practice of calling upon the saints were not adopted without protest.
10079{_}250{_}Thus we read, in contrast to the last epitaph, this simple one:--    ZOSIME VIVAS IN NOMINE XTI    O Zosimus, mayst thou live in the name of Christ!
10079{_}251{_}And again, in the strongest and most direct words:--    SOLVS DEVS ANIMAM TVAM   DEFENDAD ALEXANDRE    May God alone protect thy spirit, Alexander!
10079{_}252{_}One more inscription and we have done; it well closes the long list:--    QVI LEGERIT VIVAT IN CHRISTO    Whoever shall read this, may he live in Christ!
10079{_}253{_}As the fourth century advanced, the character of the inscriptions underwent great change.
10079{_}254{_}They become less simple; they exhibit less faith, and more worldliness; superlatives abound in them; and the want of feeling displays itself in the abundance of words.
10079{_}255{_}We end here our examinations of the testimony of the catacombs regarding the doctrine, the faith, and the lives of the Christians of Rome in the first three centuries.
10079{_}256{_}The evidence is harmonious and complete.
10079{_}257{_}It leaves no room for skepticism or doubt.
10079{_}258{_}There are no contradictions in it.
10079{_}259{_}From every point of view, theologic, historic, artistic, the results coincide and afford mutual support.
10079{_}260{_}The construction of the catacombs, the works of painting found within them, the inscriptions on the graves, all unite in bearing witness to the simplicity of the faith, the purity of the doctrine, the strength of the feeling, the change in the lives of the vast mass of the members of the early church of Christ.
10079{_}261{_}A light had come into the world, and the dark passages of the underground cemeteries were illuminated by it, and manifest its brightness.
10079{_}262{_}Wherever it reached, the world was humanized and purified.
10079{_}263{_}To the merely outward eye it might at first have seemed faint and dim, but "the kingdom of God cometh not with observation."
10079{_}264{_}THREE OF US.
10079{_}265{_}Such a spring day as it was!--the sky all one mild blue, hazy on the hills, warm with sunshine overhead; a soft south-wind, expressive, and full of new impulses, blowing up from the sea, and spreading the news of life all over our brown pastures and leaf-strewn woods.
10079{_}266{_}The crocuses in Friend Allis's garden-bed shot up cups of gold and sapphire from the dark mould; slight long buds nestled under the yellow-green leafage of the violet-patch; white and sturdy points bristled on the corner that in May was thick with lilies-of-the-valley, crisp, cool, and fragrant; and in a knotty old apricot-tree two bluebirds and a robin did heralds' duty, singing of summer's procession to come; and we made ready to receive it both in our hearts and garments.
10079{_}267{_}Josephine Boyle, Letty Allis, and I, Sarah Anderson, three cousins as we were, sat at the long window of Friend Allis's parlor, pretending to sew, really talking.
10079{_}268{_}Mr. Stepel, a German artist, had just left us; and a little trait of Miss Josephine's, that had occurred during his call, brought out this observation from Cousin Letty:--  "Jo, how could thee let down thy hair so before that man?"
10079{_}269{_}Jo laughed.
10079{_}270{_}"Thee is a little innocent, Letty, with your pretty dialect!
10079{_}271{_}Why did I let my hair down?
10079{_}272{_}For Mr. Stepel to see it, of course."
10079{_}273{_}"That is very evident," interposed I; "but Letty is not so innocent or so wise as to have done wondering at your caprices, Jo; expound, if you please, for her edification."
10079{_}274{_}"I do not pretend to be wise or simple, Sarah; but I didn't think Cousin Josephine had so much vanity."
10079{_}275{_}"You certainly shall have a preacher-bonnet, Letty.
10079{_}276{_}How do you know it was vanity, my dear?
10079{_}277{_}I saw you show Mr. Stepel your embroidery with the serenest satisfaction; now you made your crewel cherries, and I didn't make my hair; which was vain?"
10079{_}278{_}Letty was astounded.
10079{_}279{_}"Thee has a gift of speech, certainly, Jo."
10079{_}280{_}"I have a gift of honesty, you mean.
10079{_}281{_}My hair is very handsome, and I knew Mr. Stepel would admire it with real pleasure, for it is a rare color.
10079{_}282{_}I took down those curls with quite as simple an intention as you brought him that little picture of Cole's to see."
10079{_}283{_}Josephine was right,--partly, at least.
10079{_}284{_}Her hair was perfect; its tint the exact hue of a new chestnut-skin, with golden lights, and shadows of deep brown; not a tinge of red libelled it as auburn; and the light broke on its glittering waves as it does on the sea, tipping the undulations with sunshine, and scattering rays of gold through the long, loose curls, and across the curve of the massive coil, that seemed almost too heavy for her proud and delicate head to bear.
10079{_}285{_}Mr. Stepel was excusably enthusiastic about its beauty, and Jo as cool as if it had been a wig.
10079{_}286{_}Sometimes I thought this peculiar hair was an expression of her own peculiar character.
10079{_}287{_}Letty said truly that Jo had a gift of speech; and she, having said her say about the hair, dismissed the matter, with no uneasy recurring to it, and took up a book from the table, declaring she was tired of her seam;-- she always was tired of sewing!
10079{_}288{_}Presently she laughed.
10079{_}289{_}"What is it, Jo?"
10079{_}290{_}said I.
10079{_}291{_}"Why, it is 'Jane Eyre,' with Letty Allis's name on the blank leaf.
10079{_}292{_}That is what I call an anachronism, spiritually.
10079{_}293{_}What do you think about the book, Letty?"
10079{_}294{_}said she, turning her lithe figure round in the great chair toward the little Quakeress, whose pretty red head and apple-blossom of a face bloomed out of her gray attire and prim collar with a certain fascinating contrast.
10079{_}295{_}"I think it has a very good moral tendency, Cousin Jo."
10079{_}296{_}The clear, hazel eyes flashed a most amused comment at me.
10079{_}297{_}"Well, what do you call the moral, Letty?"
10079{_}298{_}"Why,--I should think,--I do not quite know that the moral is stated, Josephine,--but I think thee will allow it was a great triumph of principle for Jane Eyre to leave Mr. Rochester when she discovered that he was married."
10079{_}299{_}Jo flung herself back impatiently in the chair, and began an harangue.
10079{_}300{_}"That is a true world's judgment!
10079{_}301{_}And you, you innocent little Quaker girl!
10079{_}302{_}think it is the height of virtue not to elope with a married man, who has entirely and deliberately deceived you, and adds to the wrong of deceit the insult of proposing an elopement!
10079{_}303{_}Triumph of principle!
10079{_}304{_}I should call it the result of common decency, rather,--a thing that the instinct of any woman would compel her to do.
10079{_}305{_}My only wonder is how Jane Eyre could continue to love him."
10079{_}306{_}"My dear young friend," said I, rather grimly, "when a woman loves a man, it is apt, I regret to say, to become a fact, not a theory; and facts are stubborn things, you know.
10079{_}307{_}It is not easy to set aside a real affection."
10079{_}308{_}"I know that, ma'am," retorted Jo, in a slightly sarcastic tone; "it is a painful truth; still, I do think a deliberate deceit practised on me by any man would decapitate any love I had for him, quite inevitably."
10079{_}309{_}"So it might, in your case," replied I; "for you never will love a man, only your idea of one.
10079{_}310{_}You will go on enjoying your mighty theories and dreams till suddenly the juice of that 'little western flower' drips on your eyelids, and then I shall have the pleasure of seeing you caress 'the fair large ears' of some donkey, and hang rapturously upon its bray, till you perhaps discover that he has pretended, on your account solely, to like roses, when he has a natural proclivity to thistles; and then, pitiable child!
10079{_}311{_}you will discover what you have been caressing, and--I spare you conclusions; only, for my part, I pity the animal!
10079{_}312{_}Now Jane Eyre was a highly practical person; she knew the man she loved was only a man, and rather a bad specimen at that; she was properly indignant at this further development of his nature, but reflecting in cool blood, afterward, that it was only his nature, and finding it proper and legal to marry him, she did so, to the great satisfaction of herself and the public.
10079{_}313{_}_You_ would have made a new ideal of St. John Rivers, who was infinitely the best material of the two, and possibly gone on to your dying day in the belief that his cold and hard soul was only the adamant of the seraph, encouraged in that belief by his real and high principle,-- a thing that went for sounding brass with that worldly-wise little philosopher, Jane, because it did not act more practically on his inborn traits."
10079{_}314{_}"Bah!"
10079{_}315{_}said Josephine, "when did you turn gypsy, Sally?
10079{_}316{_}You ought to sell _dukkeripen_, and make your fortune.
10079{_}317{_}Why don't you unfold Letty's fate?"
10079{_}318{_}"No," said I, laughing.
10079{_}319{_}"Don't you know that the afflatus always exhausts the priestess?
10079{_}320{_}You may tell Letty's fortune, or mine, if you will; but my power is gone."
10079{_}321{_}"I can tell yours easily, O Sibyl!"
10079{_}322{_}replied she.
10079{_}323{_}"You will never marry, neither for real nor ideal.
10079{_}324{_}You should have fallen in love in the orthodox way, when you were seventeen.
10079{_}325{_}You are adaptive enough to have moulded yourself into any nature that you loved, and constant enough to have clung to it through good and evil.
10079{_}326{_}You would have been a model wife, and a blessed mother.
10079{_}327{_}But now--you are too old, my dear; you have seen too much; you have not hardened yourself, but you have learned to see too keenly into other people.
10079{_}328{_}You don't respect men, 'except exceptions'; and you have seen so much matrimony that is harsh and unlovable, that you dread it; and yet--Don't look at me that way, Sarah!
10079{_}329{_}I shall cry!--My dear!
10079{_}330{_}my darling!
10079{_}331{_}I did not mean to hurt you.--I am a perfect fool!--Do please look at me with your old sweet eyes again!--How could I!"
10079{_}332{_}----  "Look at Letty," said I, succeeding at last in a laugh.
10079{_}333{_}And really Letty was comical to look at; she was regarding Josephine and me with her eyes wide open like two blue larkspur flowers, her little red lips apart, and her whole pretty surface face quite full of astonishment.
10079{_}334{_}"Wasn't that a nice little tableau, Letty?"
10079{_}335{_}said Josephine, with preternatural coolness.
10079{_}336{_}"You looked so sleepy, I thought I'd wake you up with a bit of a scene from 'Lara Aboukir, the Pirate Chief'; you know we have a great deal of private theatricals at Baltimore; you should see me in that play as Flashmoria, the Bandit's Bride."
10079{_}337{_}Letty rubbed her left eye a little, as if to see whether she was sleepy or not, and looked grave; for me, the laugh came easily enough now.
10079{_}338{_}Jo saw she had not quite succeeded, so she turned the current another way.
10079{_}339{_}"Shall I tell your fortune now, Letty?
10079{_}340{_}Are you quite waked up?"
10079{_}341{_}said she.
10079{_}342{_}"No, thee needn't, Cousin Jo; thee don't tell very good ones, I think."
10079{_}343{_}"No, Letty, she shall not vex your head with nonsense.
10079{_}344{_}I think your fate is patent; you will grow on a little longer like a pink china-aster, safe in the garden, and in due time marry some good Friend,--Thomas Dugdale, very possibly,--and live a tranquil life here in Slepington till you arrive at a preacher-bonnet, and speak in meeting, as dear Aunt Allis did before you."
10079{_}345{_}Letty turned pale with rage.
10079{_}346{_}I did not think her blonde temperament held such passion.
10079{_}347{_}"I won't!
10079{_}348{_}I won't!
10079{_}349{_}I never will!"
10079{_}350{_}she cried out.
10079{_}351{_}"I hate Thomas Dugdale, Sarah!
10079{_}352{_}Thee ought to know better about me!
10079{_}353{_}thee knows I cannot endure him, the old thing!"
10079{_}354{_}This climax was too much for Jo.
10079{_}355{_}With raised brows and a round mouth, she had been on the point of whistling ever since Letty began; it was an old, naughty trick of hers; but now she laughed outright.
10079{_}356{_}"No sort of inspiration left, Sally!
10079{_}357{_}I must patch up Letty's fate myself.
10079{_}358{_}Flatter not yourself that she is going to be a good girl and marry in meeting; not she!
10079{_}359{_}If there's a wild, scatter-brained, handsome, dissipated, godless youth in all Slepington, it is on him that testy little heart will fix,--and think him not only a hero, but a prodigy of genius.
10079{_}360{_}Friend Allis will break her heart over Letty; but I'd bet you a pack of gloves, that in three years you'll see that juvenile Quakeress in a scarlet satin hat and feather, with a blue shawl, and green dress, on the arm of a fast young man with black hair, and a cigar in his mouth."
10079{_}361{_}"Why!
10079{_}362{_}where _did_ thee ever see him, Josey?"
10079{_}363{_}exclaimed Letty, now rosy with quick blushes.
10079{_}364{_}The question was irresistible.
10079{_}365{_}Jo and I burst into a peal of laughter that woke Friend Allis from her nap, and, bringing her into the parlor, forced us to recover our gravity; and presently Jo and I took leave.
10079{_}366{_}Letty was an orphan, and lived with her cousin, Friend Allis.
10079{_}367{_}I, too, was alone; but I kept a tiny house in Slepington, part of which I rented, and Jo was visiting me.
10079{_}368{_}As we walked home, along the quiet street overhung with willows and sycamores, I said to her, "Jo, how came you to know Letty's secret?"
10079{_}369{_}"My dear, I did not know it any more than you; but I drew the inference of her tastes from her character.
10079{_}370{_}She is excitable,--even passionate; but her formal training has allowed no scope for either trait, and suppression has but concentrated them.
10079{_}371{_}She really pines for some excitement;--what, then, could be more natural than that her fancy should light upon some person utterly diverse from what she is used to see?
10079{_}372{_}That is simple enough.
10079{_}373{_}I hit upon the black hair on the same principle, 'like in difference.'
10079{_}374{_}The cigar seemed wonderful to the half-frightened, all-amazed child; but who ever sees a fast young man without a cigar?"
10079{_}375{_}"I am afraid it is Henry Malden," said I, meditatively; "he is all you describe, but he is also radically bad; besides, having been in the Mexican war, he will have the prestige of a hero to Letty.
10079{_}376{_}How can the poor girl be undeceived before it is quite too late?"
10079{_}377{_}"What do you want to undeceive her for, Sally?
10079{_}378{_}Do you suppose that will prevent her marrying Mr.
10079{_}379{_}Malden?"
10079{_}380{_}"I should think so, most certainly!"
10079{_}381{_}"Not in the least.
10079{_}382{_}If you want Letty to marry him, just judiciously oppose it.
10079{_}383{_}Go to her, and say you come as a friend to tell her Mr. Malden's faults, and the result will be, she will hate you, and be deeper in love with him than ever."
10079{_}384{_}"You don't give her credit for common sense, Jo."
10079{_}385{_}"Just as much as any girl of her age has in love.
10079{_}386{_}Did you ever know a woman who gave up a man she loved because she was warned against him?--or even if she knew his character well, herself?
10079{_}387{_}I don't know but there are women who could do it, from sheer religious principle.
10079{_}388{_}I believe you might, Sarah.
10079{_}389{_}It would be a hard struggle, and wear you to a shadow in mind and body; but you have a conscience, and, for a woman with a heart as soft as pudding, the most thoroughly rigid streak of duty in you; none of which Letty has to depend on.
10079{_}390{_}No; if you want to save her, take her away from Slepington; take her to Saratoga, to Newport, to Washington; turn her small head with gayety: she is pretty enough to have a dozen lovers at any watering-place; it is only propinquity that favors Mr. Malden here."
10079{_}391{_}"I can't do that, Josephine.
10079{_}392{_}I have not the means, and Miss Allis would not have the will, even if she believed in your prescription."
10079{_}393{_}"Then Letty must stay here and bide her time.
10079{_}394{_}You believe in a special Providence, Sarah, don't you?"
10079{_}395{_}"Yes, of course I do."
10079{_}396{_}"Then cannot you leave her to that care?
10079{_}397{_}Circumstances do not work for you.
10079{_}398{_}Perhaps it is best that she should marry him, suffer, live, love, and be refined by fire."
10079{_}399{_}My heart sunk at the prospect of these possibilities.
10079{_}400{_}Josephine put her arm round me.
10079{_}401{_}"Sally," said she, in her softest tone, "I grieved you, dear, this afternoon.
10079{_}402{_}I did not mean to.
10079{_}403{_}I grieved myself most.
10079{_}404{_}Please forgive me!"
10079{_}405{_}"I haven't anything to forgive, Jo," said I.
10079{_}406{_}"What you said to me was true, painfully true,--and, being so, for a moment pained me.
10079{_}407{_}I should have been much happier to be married, I know; but now I daren't think of it.
10079{_}408{_}I have lost a great deal.
10079{_}409{_}I have   "--'lost _my_ place,   _My_ sweet, safe corner by the household fire,   Behind the heads of children';  "and yet I do not know that I have not gained a little.
10079{_}410{_}It is something, Jo, to know that I am not in the power of a bad, or even an ill-tempered man.
10079{_}411{_}I can sit by my fire and know that no one will come home to fret at me,--that I shall encounter no cold looks, no sneers, no bursts of anger, no snarl of stinginess, no contempt of my opinion and advice.
10079{_}412{_}I know that now men treat me with respect and attention, such as their wives rarely, if ever, receive from them.
10079{_}413{_}Sensitive and fastidious as I am, I do not know whether my gain is not, to me, greater than my loss.
10079{_}414{_}I know it ought not to be so,--that it argues a vicious, an unchristian, almost an uncivilized state of society; but that does not affect the facts."
10079{_}415{_}"You frighten me, Sarah.
10079{_}416{_}I cannot believe this is always true of men and their wives."
10079{_}417{_}"Neither is it.
10079{_}418{_}Some men are good and kind and gentle, gentle-men, even in their families; and every woman believes the man she is to marry is that exception.
10079{_}419{_}Jo,--bend your ear down closer,--I thought once I knew such a man,--and,--dear,--I loved him."
10079{_}420{_}"My darling!--but, Sarah, why"--  "Because, as you said, Josey, I was too old; I had seen too much; I would not give way to an impulse.
10079{_}421{_}I bent my soul to know him; I rang the metal on more than one stone, and every time it rang false.
10079{_}422{_}I knew, if I married him, I should live and die a wretched woman.
10079{_}423{_}Was it not better to live alone?"
10079{_}424{_}"But, Sarah,--if he loved you?"
10079{_}425{_}"He did not,--not enough to hurt himself; he could not love anything so much better than his ease as to suffer, Josey: he was safe.
10079{_}426{_}He thought, or said, he loved me; but he was mistaken."
10079{_}427{_}"Safe, indeed!
10079{_}428{_}He ought to have been shot!"
10079{_}429{_}"Hush, dear!"
10079{_}430{_}There was a long pause.
10079{_}431{_}It was as when you lift a wreck from the tranquil sea and let it fall again to the depths, useless to wave or shore; the black and ghastly hulk is covered; it is seen no more; but the water palpitates with circling rings, trembles above the grave, dashes quick and apprehensive billows upon the sand, and is long in regaining its quiet surface.
10079{_}432{_}"I wonder if there ever was a perfect man," said Jo, at length, drawing a deep sigh.
10079{_}433{_}"You an American girl, Jo, and don't think at once of Washington?"
10079{_}434{_}"My dear, I am bored to death with Washington _a l'Americain_.
10079{_}435{_}A man!-- how dare you call him a man?--don't you know he is a myth, an abstraction, a plaster-of-Paris cast?
10079{_}436{_}Did you ever hear any human trait of his noticed?
10079{_}437{_}Weren't you brought up to regard him as a species of special seraph, a sublime and stainless figure, inseparable from a grand manner and a scroll?
10079{_}438{_}Did you ever dare suppose he ate, or drank, or kissed his wife?
10079{_}439{_}You started then at the idea: I saw you!"
10079{_}440{_}"You are absurd, Jo.
10079{_}441{_}It is true that he is exactly, among us, what demigods were to the Greeks,--only less human than they.
10079{_}442{_}But when I once get my neck out of the school-yoke, I do not start at such suggestions as yours; I believe he did comport himself as a man of like passions with others, and was as far from being a hero to his _valet-de-chambre_ as anybody."
10079{_}443{_}By this time we were at home, and Jo flung her parasol on the bench in the porch, and sat down beside it with a gesture of weariness and disgust mingled.
10079{_}444{_}"Why will you, of all people, Sarah, quote that tinkling, superficial trash of a proverb, so palpably French, when the true reason why a man is not a hero to his lackey is only because he is seen with a lackey's eyes, --the sight of a low, convention-ridden, narrow, uneducated mind, unable to take a broad enough view to see that a man is a hero because he is a man, because he overleaps the level of his life, and is greater than his race, being one of them?
10079{_}445{_}If he were of the heroic race, what virtue in being heroic?
10079{_}446{_}it is the assertion of his trivial life that makes his speciality evident,--the shadow that throws out the bas-relief.
10079{_}447{_}We chatter endlessly about the immense good of Washington's example: I believe its good would be more than doubled, could we be made, nationally, to see him as a human being, living on 'human nature's daily food,' having mortal and natural wants, tastes, and infirmities, but building with and over all, by the help of God and a good will, the noble and lofty edifice of a patriot manhood, a pure life of duty and devotion, sublime for its very strength and simpleness, heroic because manly and human."
10079{_}448{_}The day had waned, and the sunset lit Josephine's excited eyes with fire: she was not beautiful, but now, if ever, beauty visited her with a transient caress.
10079{_}449{_}She looked up and met my eyes fixed on her.
10079{_}450{_}"What is it, Sally?--what do I look like?"
10079{_}451{_}"Very pretty, just now, Jo; your eyes are bright and your cheek flushed: the sunshine suits you.
10079{_}452{_}I admire you tonight."
10079{_}453{_}"I am glad," said she, naively.
10079{_}454{_}"I often wish to be pretty."
10079{_}455{_}"A waste wish, Jo!--and yet I have entertained it myself."
10079{_}456{_}"It's not so much matter for you, Sarah; for people love you.
10079{_}457{_}And besides, you have a certain kind of beauty: your eyes are beautiful,--rather too sad, perhaps, but fine in shape and tint; and you have a good head, and a delicately outlined face.
10079{_}458{_}Moreover, you are picturesque: people look at you, and then look again,--and, any way, love you, don't they?"
10079{_}459{_}"People are very good to me, Jo."
10079{_}460{_}"Oh, yes!
10079{_}461{_}we all know that people as a mass are kindly, considerate, and unselfish; that they are given to loving and admiring disagreeable and ugly people; in short, that the millennium has come.
10079{_}462{_}Sally, my dear, you are a small hypocrite,--or else--But I think we won't establish a mutual- admiration society to-night, as there are only two of us; besides, I am hungry: let us have tea."
10079{_}463{_}The next day, Josephine left me.
10079{_}464{_}As we walked together toward the landing of the steamer, Letty Allis emerged from a green lane to say good-bye, and down its vista I discerned the handsome, lazy person of Henry Malden, but I did not inform Letty of my discovery.
10079{_}465{_}A year passed away,--to me with the old monotonous routine; full of work, not wanting in solace; barren, indeed, of household enjoyments and vicissitudes; solitary, sometimes desolate, yet peaceful even in monotony.
10079{_}466{_}But this new spring had not come with such serene neglect to the other two of us three.
10079{_}467{_}Against advice, remonstrance, and entreaty from her good friends, Letty Allis had married Henry Malden, and, in attire more tasteful, but quite as far from Quakerism as Josephine had predicted, beamed upon the inhabitants of Slepington from the bow-window, or open door, of a cottage very _ornee_ indeed; while the odor of a tolerable cigar served as Mr. Malden's exponent, wherever he abode.
10079{_}468{_}And to Josephine had come a loss no annual resurrection should repair: her mother was dead; she, too, was orphaned,--for she had never known her father; her only sister was married far away; and I kept an old promise in going to her for a year's stay at least.
10079{_}469{_}Aunt Boyle's property had consisted chiefly in large cotton mills owned by herself and her twin brother,--who, dying before her, left her all his own share in them.
10079{_}470{_}These mills were on a noisy little river in the western part of Massachusetts,--in a valley, narrow, but picturesque, and so far above the level of the sea that the air was keen and pure as among mountains.
10079{_}471{_}Mrs. Boyle had removed here from Baltimore, a few years before her own death, that she might be with her brother through his long and fatal illness; and, finding her health improved by change of air, had occupied his house ever since, until one of those typhoid fevers that infest such river-gorges at certain seasons of the year entered the village about the mills, when, in visiting the sick, she took the epidemic herself and died.
10079{_}472{_}Josephine still retained the house endeared to her by sad and glad recollections; and it was there I found her, when, after renting the whole of my little tenement at Slepington, I betook myself to Valley Mills at her request.
10079{_}473{_}The cottage where she lived was capacious enough for her wants, and though plain, even to an air of superciliousness, without, was most luxurious within,--made to use and live in; for Mr. Brown, her uncle, was an Englishman, and had never arrived at that height of Transatlantic _ton_ which consists in shrouding and darkening all the pleasant rooms in the house, and skulking through life in the basement and attic.
10079{_}474{_}Sunshine, cushions, and flowers were Mr. Brown's personal tastes; and plenty of these characterized the cottage.
10079{_}475{_}A green terrace between hill and river spread out before the door for lawn and garden, and a tiny conservatory abutted upon the brink of the terrace slope, from a bay-window in the library, that opened sidewise into this winter-garden.
10079{_}476{_}I found Jo more changed than I had expected: this last year of country life had given strength and elasticity to the tall and slender figure; a steady rose of health burned on either cheek; and sorrow had subdued and calmed her quick spirits.
10079{_}477{_}I was at home directly, and a sweeter summer never glowed and blushed over earth than that which installed me in the Nook Cottage.
10079{_}478{_}Out of doors the whole country was beautiful, and attainable; within, I had continual resources in my usual work and in Jo's society: for she was one of those persons who never are uninteresting, never fatiguing; a certain salient charm pervaded her conversation, and a simplicity quite original startled you continually in her manner and ways.
10079{_}479{_}I liked to watch her about the house; dainty and fastidious in the extreme about some things, utterly careless about others, you never knew where or when either trait would show itself next.
10079{_}480{_}She was scrupulous as to the serving of meals, for instance,--almost to a fault; no carelessness, no slight neglect, was admitted here, and always on the spotless damask laid with quaint china stood a tapered vase of white Venice glass, with one, or two, or three blossoms, sometimes a cluster of leaves, the spray of a wild vine, or the tasselled branch of a larch-tree jewelled with rose-red cones, arranged therein with an artist's taste and skill: but perhaps, while she sharply rebuked the maid for a dim spot on her chocolate-pitcher or a grain of sugar spilt on the salver, her white India shawl lay trailed over the divan half upon the floor, and her gloves fluttered on the doorstep till the wind carried them off to find her parasol hanging in the honeysuckle boughs.
10079{_}481{_}But, happily, it is not one's duty to make other people uncomfortable by perpetually tinkering at that trait in them which most offends our own nature; and I thought it more for my good and hers to learn patience myself than undertake to beat her into order; the result of which was peace and good-will that vindicated my wisdom to myself; and I found her, faults and all, sufficiently fascinating and lovable.
10079{_}482{_}A year passed away serenely; and when spring came again, Josephine refused to let me leave her.
10079{_}483{_}Our life was quiet enough, but, with such beautiful Nature, and plenty to do, we were not lonely,--less so because Jo's hands were as open as her heart, and to her all the sick and poor looked, not only for help, but for the rarer consolations of living sympathy and counsel.
10079{_}484{_}Her shrewd common sense, her practical capacity, her kindly, cheerful face, her power of appreciating a position of want and perplexity and seeing the best way out of it, and, above all, her deep and fervent religious feeling, made her an invaluable friend to just that class who most needed her.
10079{_}485{_}In the course of this spring we gained an addition to our society, in the person of Mr. Waring, the son of the gentleman who had bought the mills at Mrs. Boyle's death, but who had hitherto conducted them by an overseer.
10079{_}486{_}He had recently bought a little island in the middle of the river, just below the dam, and proposed erecting a new mill upon it; but as the Tunxis (the Indian name of our river) was liable to rapid and destructive freshets, the mill required a deep and secure foundation and a lower story of stone.
10079{_}487{_}This implied some skilful engineering, and Mr. Arthur Waring, having studied this subject fully abroad, came on from Boston, and took up his abode in Valley Mills village.
10079{_}488{_}Of course, we being his only hope of society in the place, he made our acquaintance early.
10079{_}489{_}I rather liked him; his manner was good, his perceptions acute, his tastes refined, and he had a certain strength of will that gave force to a character otherwise common-place.
10079{_}490{_}Josephine liked him at once; she laid his shyness and _brusquerie_, which were only the expression of a dominant self- consciousness, to genuine modesty.
10079{_}491{_}He was depressed and moody, because he was bored for want of acquaintance, and missed the adulation and caresses that he received at home as an only child; but Jo's swift imagination painted this as the trait of a reflective and melancholy nature disgusted with the world, and pitied him accordingly; a mild way of misanthropic speech, that is apt to infest young men, added to this delusion; and, with all the energy of her sweet, earnest disposition, Josephine undertook his education,--undertook to teach him faith and hope and charity, to set right his wayward soul, to renovate his bitter opinions, to make him a better and a happier man.
10079{_}492{_}It is a well-known fact in the philosophy of the human mind, that it is apt to gain more by imparting than by receiving; and since philosophy, where it becomes fact, does not mercifully adjust its results to circumstance, but rushes on in implacable grooves, and clears its own track of whatever lies thereon by the summary process of crushing it to dust, it did not pause now for the pure intentions and tender heart which, in teaching another love to men, taught herself love to a man, and learnt far better than her pupil.
10079{_}493{_}Mr. Waring was but a man; he did not love Josephine,--he admired her; he loved nothing but himself, his quiet, his pleasure; and while she ministered to either, he regarded her with a species of affection that put on the mask of a diviner passion and used its language.
10079{_}494{_}A thousand little things showed the man fully to me, a cool spectator; but she who needed most the discerning eye regarded this gay bubble as if it had been a jewel.
10079{_}495{_}Perhaps I blame him too severely, for it was against the very heart of my heart that he sinned; possibly I do not allow for the temptation it was to a young man, quite alone in a country village, without resources, and accustomed to the flattery and caresses of a devoted mother, to find himself agreeable in the eyes of a noble and lovable woman.
10079{_}496{_}Possibly, in his place, a better man might have sought her society, drawn her out of her reserve for his own delectation, confided in her, worked upon her pity, claimed her care, played on her simplicity and ignorance of the world, crept into her heart and won its strength of emotion and its generous affection,--in short, made love to her, without saying so, honestly and openly.
10079{_}497{_}Yet there are some men who would not have done it; and even yet, while I try to regard Arthur Waring with Christian charity, I feel that I cannot trust him, that I do not respect him,--that, if I dared despise anything God has made, my first contempt would light on him.
10079{_}498{_}In the autumn, while all this was going on, I received a painful and wretched letter from Letty Malden, begging me to come to her.
10079{_}499{_}I could not resist such an appeal; and one of Josephine's little nieces having come to spend the winter with her, I hurried to Slepington,--not, I am sure, in the least regretted by Mr. Waring, who had begun to look at me with uneasy and sometimes defiant eyes.
10079{_}500{_}I found a miserable household here.
10079{_}501{_}Mr. Malden had in no way reformed.
10079{_}502{_}When did marriage ever reform a bad man?
10079{_}503{_}On the contrary, he was more dissipated than ever; and whenever he came home, the welcome that waited for him was one little calculated to make home pleasant; for Letty's quick temper blazed up in reproach and reviling that drew out worse recrimination; and even the little, wailing, feeble baby, that filled Letty's arms and consoled her in his absence, was only further cause of strife between her and her husband.
10079{_}504{_}Often, as I came down the street and saw the pretty outside of the cottage, waving with creepers, and hedged about with thorns, whose gay berries decked it as if for a festival, I thought of what a good old preacher among the Friends once said to me: "Sarah, thee will live to find shows are often seems; thee sees many a quiet house, with gay windows, that is hell inside."
10079{_}505{_}I soon found that I must stay all winter at Slepington.
10079{_}506{_}I had a hard task before me,--to try and teach Letty that she had no right to neglect her own duties because her husband ignored his.
10079{_}507{_}But six months of continual dropping seemed to wear a tiny channel of perception; and my presence, as well as the efforts we made together to preserve order, if not serenity, in the house, restored a certain dim hope to Letty's mind, and I began to see that the "purification by fire" was doing its work, in slow pain, but to a sure end.
10079{_}508{_}Selfish as it was, I cannot say that I felt sorry to return to Jo, who wrote for me in April, urging me to come as soon as I could, for Mr. Waring had fallen from the mill-wall and broken his leg, and the workmen, in their confusion, had carried him to her house, and she wanted me to help her.
10079{_}509{_}I learned, on reaching Valley Mills, that the new building on the island had not been completed far enough to resist a heavy freshet, that had swept away part of the first story, where the mortar was not yet hardened; and it was in traversing these wet stones to ascertain the extent of the damage that Mr. Waring had slipped, and, unable to recover his footing, fallen on a heap of stones and received his injury.
10079{_}510{_}My first question to Josephine was, "Where is Mr. Waring's mother?"
10079{_}511{_}"He would not send for her, Sally," said she, "because she is not well, and he feared to startle her."
10079{_}512{_}"H'm!"
10079{_}513{_}said I, very curtly.
10079{_}514{_}Josephine looked at me with innocent, grave eyes,--dear, simple child!-- and yet, for anybody but herself she would have been sufficiently discerning.
10079{_}515{_}This love seemed to have remodelled her nature, to have taken from her all the serpent's wisdom, to have destroyed her common sense, and distorted her view of everything in which Arthur Waring was concerned.
10079{_}516{_}She had certainly got on very fast in my absence.
10079{_}517{_}I had returned too late.
10079{_}518{_}I had little to do with the care of the invalid; that devolved on Jo; my offers of service were kindly received, but always declined.
10079{_}519{_}Nobody could read to him so well as Miss Boyle.
10079{_}520{_}Nobody else understood his moods, his humors, his whims; she knew his tastes with ominous exactness.
10079{_}521{_}It was she who arranged his meals on the salver with such care and grace, nay, even cooked them at times; for Jo believed, like a rational woman, that intellect and cultivation increase one's capacity for every office,--that a woman of intelligence should be able to excel an ignorant servant in every household duty, by just so much as she excels her in mind.
10079{_}522{_}In fact, this was a pleasant life to two persons, but harassing enough for me.
10079{_}523{_}Had I been confident of Arthur Waring's integrity, I should have regarded him with friendly and cordial interest; but I had every reason to distrust him.
10079{_}524{_}I perceived he had so far insinuated himself into Jo's confidence, that his whole artillery of expressive looks, broken sentences, even caresses, were received by her with entire good faith; but when I asked her seriously if I was to regard Mr. Waring as her lover, she burst into indignant denial, colored scarlet, and was half inclined to be angry with me,--though a certain tremulous key, into which her usually sweet and steady voice broke while she declared he had never spoken to her of love, it was only friendship, witnessed against her that she was apprehensive, sad, perhaps visited with a tinge of that causeless shame which even in a pure and good woman conventionality constrains, when she has loved a man before he says in plain English, "I love you," though every act and look and tone of his may have carried that significance unmistakably for years.
10079{_}525{_}Thank God, there is a day of sure judgment coming, when conventions and shields of usage will save no man from the due vengeance of truth upon falsehood, justice upon smooth and plausible duplicity!
10079{_}526{_}In due time Mr. Waring recovered.
10079{_}527{_}If there was any change in his manner to Jo, it was too slight to be seen, though it was felt, and was, after all, the carelessness of a person certain of his foothold in her good graces, rather than the evident withdrawal of attention,--which I could have pardoned even then, had it been the result of honest regret for past carelessness, and stern resolution to repair that past.
