10079{_}528{_}Whatever it was, Jo perceived that her ideal man was become a real man; but, with a tenacity of nature, for which in my fate-telling I had not given her credit, she was as constant to the substance as she had been to the dream; and while she lost both health and spirits in the contemplation of Arthur Waring's fitful and heedless manner toward her, and was evidently pained by the discovery of his selfish and politic traits,--to call them by no harsher name,--it was inexpressibly touching to hear the excuses she made for him, to see the all-shielding love with which she veiled his faults, and kept him as a mother would keep her graceless, yet dearest child from animadversion and reproach.
10079{_}529{_}In the mean time I heard often from Letty,--no good news of her husband, but that her child grew more and more a comfort, that her friends were very kind, and always in a tiny postscript some such phrase as this: "I try to be patient, Sarah," or "I don't scold Harry so much as I did, dear."
10079{_}530{_}I hoped for Letty, for she persevered.
10079{_}531{_}That summer we saw less than ever of Mr. Waring; he was very busy at the mill in order that it might be far enough advanced to resist the inevitable spring freshets; and besides, we were absent from the Valley some weeks, endeavoring to recruit Jo's failing health at the sea-side.
10079{_}532{_}But this was a vain endeavor; that which sapped the springs of her life was past outward cure.
10079{_}533{_}She inherited her father's delicate and unreliable constitution, and a nervous organization, whose worst disease is ever the preying of doubt, anxiety, or regret.
10079{_}534{_}As winter drew on, she grew no better; a dim, dreamy abstraction brooded over her.
10079{_}535{_}She said to me often, with a vague alarm, "Sally, how far off you seem!
10079{_}536{_}Do come nearer!"
10079{_}537{_}She ceased to talk when we were alone, her step grew languid, her eye deeper, --and its bright expression, when you roused her, was longer in shooting back into the clouded sphere than ever before.
10079{_}538{_}She sat for hours by the window, her lovely head resting on its casement, looking out, always out and away, beyond the hills, into the deep spaces of blue air, past cloud and vapor, to the stars.
10079{_}539{_}Sudden noises startled her to an extreme degree; a quick step flushed her cheek with fire and fluttered her breath.
10079{_}540{_}How I longed for spring!
10079{_}541{_}I hoped all from the delicate ministrations of Nature; though the physician we called gave me no hope of her final recovery.
10079{_}542{_}Mr. Waring himself seemed struck with her aspect, and many little signs of friendly interest came from him.
10079{_}543{_}As often as he could, he returned to his old haunts; and while the pleasure of his presence and the excitement of his undisguised anxiety wrought on her, Jo became almost her old self for the moment, gay, cheerful, blooming,--alas!
10079{_}544{_}with the bloom of feverishness and vain hope.
10079{_}545{_}So spring drew near.
10079{_}546{_}The mill was nearly finished.
10079{_}547{_}One day in March a warm south-wind "quieted the earth" after a long rain, the river began to stir, its mail of ice to crack and heave under the sun's rays.
10079{_}548{_}I persuaded Jo to take a little drive, and once in the carriage the air reanimated her; she rested against me and talked more than I had known her for weeks.
10079{_}549{_}"What a lovely day!"
10079{_}550{_}said she; "how balmy the air is!
10079{_}551{_}there is such an expression of rest without despair, such calm expectation!
10079{_}552{_}I always think of heaven such days, Sally!--they are like the long sob with which a child finishes weeping.
10079{_}553{_}Only to think of never more knowing tears!--that is life indeed!"
10079{_}554{_}A keen pang pierced me at the vibration of her voice as she spoke.
10079{_}555{_}I thought to soothe her a little, and said, "Heaven can be no more than love, Jo, and we have a great deal of that on earth."
10079{_}556{_}"Do we?"
10079{_}557{_}answered she, in a tone of grief just tipped with irony,--and then went on: "I believe you love me, Sally.
10079{_}558{_}I would trust you with--my heart, if need were.
10079{_}559{_}I think you love me better than any one on earth does."
10079{_}560{_}"I love you enough, dear," said I; more words would have choked me in the utterance.
10079{_}561{_}Soon we turned homeward.
10079{_}562{_}"Tell John to drive down by the river," said Josephine,--"I want to see the new mill."
10079{_}563{_}"But you cannot see it from the road, Jo; the hemlocks stand between."
10079{_}564{_}"Never mind, Sally; I shall just walk through them; don't deny me!
10079{_}565{_}I want to see it all again; and perhaps the arbutus is in bloom."
10079{_}566{_}"Not yet, Jo."
10079{_}567{_}"I can get some buds, then; I want to have some just once."
10079{_}568{_}We left the carriage, and on my arm Jo strolled through the little thicket of hemlock-trees, green and fragrant.
10079{_}569{_}She seemed unusually strong.
10079{_}570{_}I began to hope.
10079{_}571{_}After much searching, we found the budded flowers; she loved most of all wild blossoms; no scent breathed from the closed petals; they were not yet kissed by the odor-giving south-wind into life and expression; but Jo looked at them with sad, far-reaching eyes.
10079{_}572{_}I think she silently said good-bye to them.
10079{_}573{_}Presently we came out on the steep bank of the river, directly opposite the mill.
10079{_}574{_}A heavy timber was thrown across from the shore to the island, on which the workmen from the west side had passed and repassed; it was firm enough for its purpose, but now, wet with the morning's rain, and high above the grinding ice, it seemed a hazardous bridge.
10079{_}575{_}As we stood looking over at the new mill, listening to the slight stir within it, apparently the setting to rights by some lingering workman of such odds and ends as remain after finishing the great whole of such a building, suddenly the cool wind, which had shifted to the north, brought on its waft a most portentous roar.
10079{_}576{_}We stood still to listen.
10079{_}577{_}Nearer and nearer it swelled, crashing and hissing as it approached.
10079{_}578{_}Josephine grasped my arm with convulsive energy, and at that instant we perceived Mr. Waring's plaid cap pass an open casement.
10079{_}579{_}She turned upon me like a wild creature driven to bay.
10079{_}580{_}I looked up-stream;--the ice had gathered in one high barrier mixed with flood-wood and timber, and, bearing above all the uprooted trunk of a huge sycamore, was coming down upon the dam like a battering-ram.
10079{_}581{_}Jo gasped.
10079{_}582{_}"The river is broken up and Arthur is on the island," said she, in a fearfully suppressed tone, and, swifter than I could think or guess her meaning, she had reached the timber, she was on it,--and with light, untrembling steps half across, when both she and I simultaneously caught sight of Mr. Waring running for dear life to the other and stronger bridge.
10079{_}583{_}Jo turned to come back; but the excitement was past that had sustained her; she trembled, she tottered.
10079{_}584{_}I ran to meet and aid her.
10079{_}585{_}Just then the roots of the great sycamore thundered against the dam; the already heavily pressed structure gave way; with the freed roar of a hurricane, the barrier, the dam, the foot-bridge swept down toward us.
10079{_}586{_}She had all but reached the end of the timber,--I stood there to grasp her hand,--when the old tree, whirled down by the torrent, struck the other end of the beam and threw Josephine forward to the bank, dashing her throbbing, panting breast, with all the force of her fall, against the hard ground.
10079{_}587{_}I lifted her in my arms.
10079{_}588{_}She was white with pain.
10079{_}589{_}Presently she opened her eyes and looked up, a flush of rapture glowed all over her face, and then the awful mist of death, gray and rigid, veiled it.
10079{_}590{_}Her head dropped on my shoulder; a sharp cry and a rush of scarlet blood passed her lips together; the head lay more heavily,--she was dead.
10079{_}591{_}But Arthur Waring never knew how or for what she died!
10079{_}592{_}Five years have passed since that day.
10079{_}593{_}Still I live at Nook Cottage; but not alone.
10079{_}594{_}Of us three, Josephine is in heaven.
10079{_}595{_}Letty is still troubled upon earth; her husband tests her patience and her temper every hour, but both temper and patience are in good training; and if ever Henry Malden is reclaimed, as I begin to see reasons to hope he will be, he will owe it to the continual example and gentle goodness of his wife, who has grown from a petulant, thoughtless girl into a lovely, unselfish, religious woman, a devoted mother and wife, "refined by fire."
10079{_}596{_}For me, the last,--whenever now I say, as I used to say, "Three of us," I mean a new three,--Paul, baby, and me; for Jo was not a prophet.
10079{_}597{_}Four years ago, while my heart- ache for her was fresh and torturing, a new pastor came to the little village church of Valley Mills.
10079{_}598{_}Mr. Lyman was very good; I have seen other men with as fine natural traits, but I have never seen a man or woman so entirely good.
10079{_}599{_}He came to me to console me; for he, too, had just lost a sister, and in listening to his story I for a moment forgot my own, as he meant I should.
10079{_}600{_}But I did not love him,--no, not till I discovered, months afterward, that he suffered incessantly from ill-health, and was all alone in the world.
10079{_}601{_}I was too much a woman to resist such a plea.
10079{_}602{_}I pitied him; I tried to take care of him; and when he asked me if I liked the office of sick-nurse, I told him I liked it well enough to wish it were for life; and now, when he wants to light my eyes out of that dreamy expression that tells him I am re-living the past, and thinking of the dead, he tells me, for the sake of the flash that follows, that I offered myself to him!
10079{_}603{_}Perhaps I did.
10079{_}604{_}But he is well now; the air of the Tunxis hills, and the rest of a quiet life, partly, I hope, good care also, have restored to him his lost health.
10079{_}605{_}And I am what Jo said I should have been,--a blessed mother, as well as a happy wife.
10079{_}606{_}The baby that lies across my lap has traits that endear her to me doubly,--traits of each of us three cousins: Josephine's hair on her little nestling head, Letty's apple-blossom complexion, and my eyes, except that they are serene when they are not smiling.
10079{_}607{_}I ask only of the love that has given me all this unexpected joy, that my little Jo may have one better trait,--her father's heart; a stronger, tenderer, and purer heart than belonged to any one among "Three of us!"
10079{_}608{_}WHAT A WRETCHED WOMAN SAID TO ME.
10079{_}609{_}All the broad East was laced with tender rings   Of widening light; the Daybreak shone afar; Deep in the hollow, 'twixt her fiery wings,   Fluttered the morning star.
10079{_}610{_}A cloud, that through the time of darkness went   With wanton winds, now, heavy-hearted, came And fell upon the sunshine, penitent,   And burning up with shame.
10079{_}611{_}The grass was wet with dew; the sheep-fields lay   Lapping together far as eye could see; And the great harvest hung the golden way   Of Nature's charity.
10079{_}612{_}My house was full of comfort; I was propped   With life's delights, all sweet as they could be, When at my door a wretched woman stopped,   And, weeping, said to me,--  "Its rose-root in youth's seasonable hours   Love in thy bosom set, so blest wert thou; Hence all the pretty little red-mouthed flowers   That climb and kiss thee now!
10079{_}613{_}"_I_ loved, but _I_ must stifle Nature's cries   With old dry blood, else perish, I was told; Hence the young light shrunk up within my eyes,   And left them blank and bold.
10079{_}614{_}"I take my deeds, all, bad as they have been,--   The way was dark, the awful pitfall bare;-- In my weak hands, up through the fires of sin,   I hold them for my prayer."
10079{_}615{_}"The thick, tough husk of evil grows about   Each soul that lives," I mused, "but doth it kill?
10079{_}616{_}When the tree rots, the imprisoned wedge falls out,   Rusted, but iron still.
10079{_}617{_}"Shall He who to the daisy has access,   Reaching it down its little lamp of dew To light it up through earth, do any less,   Last and best work, for you?"
10079{_}618{_}SONGS OF THE SEA.
10079{_}619{_}Not Dibdin's; not Barry Cornwall's; not Tom Campbell's; not any of the "Pirate's Serenades" and "I'm afloats!"
10079{_}620{_}which appear in the music-shop- windows, illustrated by lithographic vignettes of impossible ships in impracticable positions.
10079{_}621{_}These are sung by landsmen yachting in still waters and in sight of green fields, by romantic young ladies in comfortable and unmoving drawing-rooms to the tinkling of Chickering's pianos.
10079{_}622{_}What are the songs the sailor sings to the accompaniment of the thrilling shrouds, the booming double-bass of the hollow topsails, and the multitudinous chorus of Ocean?
10079{_}623{_}What does the coaster, in his brief walk "three steps and overboard," hum to himself, as he tramps up and down his little deck through the swathing mists of a Bank fog?
10079{_}624{_}What sings the cook at the galley-fire in doleful unison with the bubble of his coppers?
10079{_}625{_}Surely not songs that exult in the life of the sea.
10079{_}626{_}Certainly not, my amateur friend, anything that breathes of mastery over the elements.
10079{_}627{_}The sea is a real thing to him.
10079{_}628{_}He never is familiar with it, or thinks of it or speaks of it as his slave.
10079{_}629{_}It is "a steed that knows his rider," and, like many another steed which the men of the forecastle have mounted, knows that it can throw its rider at pleasure, and the riders know it too.
10079{_}630{_}Now and then a sailor will utter some fierce imprecation upon wind or sea, but it is in the impotence of despair, and not in the conscious, boastful mastery which the land-songs attribute to him.
10079{_}631{_}What, then, does the sailor sing?--and does he sing at all?
10079{_}632{_}Certainly the sailor sings.
10079{_}633{_}Did you ever walk through Ann Street, Boston, or haunt the purlieus of the Fulton Market?
10079{_}634{_}and when there did you never espy a huckster's board covered with little slips of printed paper of the size and shape of the bills-of-fare at the Commonwealth Hotel?
10079{_}635{_}They are printed on much coarser paper, and are by no means as typographically exact as the aforesaid _carte_, or as this page of the "Atlantic Monthly," but they are what the sailor sings.
10079{_}636{_}I know they are there, for I once spent a long summer's day in the former place, searching those files for a copy of the delightful ballad sung (or attempted to be sung) by Dick Fletcher in Scott's "Pirate,"--the ballad beginning    "It was a ship, and a ship of fame,   Launched off the stocks, bound for the main."
10079{_}637{_}I did not find my ballad, and to this day remain in ignorance of what fate befell the "hundred and fifty brisk young men" therein commemorated.
10079{_}638{_}But I found what the sailor does sing.
10079{_}639{_}It was a miscellaneous collection of sentimental songs, the worn-out rags of the stage and the parlor, or ditties of highwaymen, or ballad narratives of young women who ran away from a rich "parient" with "silvier and gold" to follow the sea.
10079{_}640{_}The truth of the story was generally established by the expedient of putting the damsel's name in the last verse,--delicately suppressing all but the initial and final letters.
10079{_}641{_}The only sea-songs that I remember were other ballads descriptive of piracies, of murders by cruel captains, and of mutinies, with a sprinkling of sea-fights dating from the last war with England.
10079{_}642{_}The point of remark is, that all of these depend for their interest upon a human association.
10079{_}643{_}Not one of them professes any concern with the sea or ships for their own sake.
10079{_}644{_}The sea is a sad, solemn reality, the theatre upon which the seaman acts his life's tragedy.
10079{_}645{_}It has no more of enchantment to him than the "magic fairy palace" of the ballet has to a scene-shifter.
10079{_}646{_}But other songs the sailor sings.
10079{_}647{_}The Mediterranean sailor is popularly supposed to chant snatches of opera over his fishing-nets; but, after all, his is only a larger sort of lake, with water of a questionable saltness.
10079{_}648{_}It can furnish dangerous enough storms upon occasion, and, far worse than storms, the terrible white-squall which lies ambushed under sunny skies, and leaps unawares upon the doomed vessel.
10079{_}649{_}But the Mediterranean is not the deep sea, nor has it produced the best and boldest navigators.
10079{_}650{_}Therefore, although we still seek the sources of our maritime law amid the rock-poised huts (once palaces) of Amalfi, we must go elsewhere for our true sea-songs.
10079{_}651{_}The sailor does not lack for singing.
10079{_}652{_}He sings at certain parts of his work;--indeed, he must sing, if he would work.
10079{_}653{_}On vessels of war, the drum and fife or boatswain's whistle furnish the necessary movement-regulator.
10079{_}654{_}There, where the strength of one or two hundred men can be applied to one and the same effort, the labor is not intermittent, but continuous.
10079{_}655{_}The men form on either side of the rope to be hauled, and walk away with it like firemen marching with their engine.
10079{_}656{_}When the headmost pair bring up at the stern or bow, they part, and the two streams flow back to the starting-point, outside the following files.
10079{_}657{_}Thus in this perpetual "follow-my-leader" way the work is done, with more precision and steadiness than in the merchant-service.
10079{_}658{_}Merchant-men are invariably manned with the least possible number, and often go to sea shorthanded, even according to the parsimonious calculations of their owners.
10079{_}659{_}The only way the heavier work can be done at all is by each man doing his utmost at the same moment.
10079{_}660{_}This is regulated by the song.
10079{_}661{_}And here is the true singing of the deep sea.
10079{_}662{_}It is not recreation; it is an essential part of the work.
10079{_}663{_}It mastheads the topsail-yards, on making sail; it starts the anchor from the domestic or foreign mud; it "rides down the main tack with a will"; it breaks out and takes on board cargo; it keeps the pumps (the ship's,--not the sailor's) going.
10079{_}664{_}A good voice and a new and stirring chorus are worth an extra man.
10079{_}665{_}And there is plenty of need of both.
10079{_}666{_}I remember well one black night in the mid-Atlantic, when we were beating up against a stiff breeze, coming on deck near midnight, just as the ship was put about.
10079{_}667{_}When a ship is tacking, the tacks and sheets (ropes which confine the clews or lower corners of the sails) are let run, in order that the yards may be swung round to meet the altered position of the ship.
10079{_}668{_}They must then be hauled taut again, and belayed, or secured, in order to keep the sails in their place and to prevent them from shaking.
10079{_}669{_}When the ship's head comes up in the wind, the sail is for a moment or two edgewise to it, and then is the nice moment, as soon as the head-sails fairly fill, when the main-yard and the yards above it can be swung readily, and the tacks and sheets hauled in.
10079{_}670{_}If the crew are too few in number, or too slow at their work, and the sails get fairly filled on the new tack, it is a fatiguing piece of work enough to "board" the tacks and sheets, as it is called.
10079{_}671{_}You are pulling at one end of the rope, but the gale is tugging at the other.
10079{_}672{_}The advantages of lungs are all against you, and perhaps the only thing to be done is to put the helm down a little, and set the sails shaking again before they can be trimmed properly.--It was just at such a time that I came on deck, as above mentioned.
10079{_}673{_}Being near eight bells, the watch on deck had been not over spry; and the consequence was that our big main-course was slatting and flying out overhead with a might that shook the ship from stem to stern.
10079{_}674{_}The flaps of the mad canvas were like successive thumps of a giant's fist upon a mighty drum.
10079{_}675{_}The sheets were jerking at the belaying-pins, the blocks rattling in sharp snappings like castanets.
10079{_}676{_}You could hear the hiss and seething of the sea alongside, and see it flash by in sudden white patches of phosphorescent foam, while all overhead was black with the flying scud.
10079{_}677{_}The English second-mate was stamping with vexation, and, with all his ills misplaced, storming at the men:--"'An'somely the weather main- brace,--'an'somely, I tell you!--'Alf a dozen of you clap on to the main sheet here,--down with 'im!--D'y'see 'ere's hall like a midshipman's bag,--heverythink huppermost and nothing 'andy.--'Aul 'im in, Hi say!"
10079{_}678{_}--But the sail wouldn't come, though.
10079{_}679{_}All the most forcible expressions of the Commination-Service were liberally bestowed on the watch.
10079{_}680{_}"Give us the song, men!"
10079{_}681{_}sang out the mate, at last,--"pull with a will!
10079{_}682{_}--together, men!--haltogether now!"
10079{_}683{_}--And then a cracked, melancholy voice struck up this chant:    "Oh, the bowline, bully bully bowline,   Oh, the bowline, bowline, HAUL!"
10079{_}684{_}At the last word every man threw his whole strength into the pull,--all singing it in chorus, with a quick, explosive sound.
10079{_}685{_}And so, jump by jump, the sheet was at last hauled taut.--I dare say this will seem very much spun out to a seafarer, but landsmen like to hear of the sea and its ways; and as more landsmen than seamen, probably, read the "Atlantic Monthly," I have told them of one genuine sea-song, and its time and place.
10079{_}686{_}Then there are pumping-songs.
10079{_}687{_}"The dismal sound of the pumps is heard," says Mr. Webster's Plymouth-Rock Oration; but being a part of the daily morning duty of a well-disciplined merchant-vessel,--just a few minutes' spell to keep the vessel free and cargo unharmed by bilge-water,--it is not a dismal sound at all, but rather a lively one.
10079{_}688{_}It was a favorite amusement with us passengers on board the ---- to go forward about pumping-time to the break of the deck and listen.
10079{_}689{_}Any quick tune to which you might work a fire-engine will serve for the music, and the words were varied with every fancy.
10079{_}690{_}"Pay me the money down," was one favorite chorus, and the verse ran thus:--    _Solo._ Your money, young man, is no object to me.
10079{_}691{_}_Chorus._ Pay me the money down!
10079{_}692{_}_Solo._ Half a crown's no great amount.
10079{_}693{_}_Chorus._ Pay me the money down!
10079{_}694{_}_Solo and Chorus.
10079{_}695{_}(Bis)_ Money down, money down, pay me the money down!
10079{_}696{_}Not much sense in all this, but it served to man and move the brakes merrily.
10079{_}697{_}Then there were other choruses, which were heard from time to time,--"And the young gals goes a-weepin',"--"O long storm, storm along stormy"; but the favorite tune was "Money down," at least with our crew.
10079{_}698{_}They were not an avaricious set, either; for their parting ceremony, on embarking, was to pitch the last half-dollars of their advance on to the wharf, to be scrambled for by the land-sharks.
10079{_}699{_}But "Money down" was the standing chorus.
10079{_}700{_}I once heard, though not on board that ship, the lively chorus of "Off she goes, and off she must go,"--    "Highland day and off she goes,   Off she goes with a flying fore-topsail,   Highland day and off she goes."
10079{_}701{_}It is one of the most spirited things imaginable, when well sung, and, when applied to the topsail-halyards, brings the yards up in grand style.
10079{_}702{_}These are some of the working-songs of the sea.
10079{_}703{_}They are not chosen for their sense, but for their sound.
10079{_}704{_}They must contain good mouth-filling words, with the vowels in the right place, and the rhythmic ictus at proper distances for chest and hand to keep true time.
10079{_}705{_}And this is why the seaman beats the wind in a trial of strength.
10079{_}706{_}The wind may whistle, but it cannot sing.
10079{_}707{_}The sailor does not whistle, on shipboard at least, but does sing.
10079{_}708{_}Besides the working-day songs, there are others for the forecastle and dog-watches, which have been already described.
10079{_}709{_}But they are seldom of the parlor pattern.
10079{_}710{_}I remember one lovely moonlight evening, off the Irish coast, when our ship was slipping along before a light westerly air,--just enough of it for everything to draw, and the ship as steady as Ailsa Crag, so that everybody got on deck, even the chronically sea-sick passengers of the steerage.
10079{_}711{_}There was a boy on board, a steerage passenger, who had been back and forth several times on this Liverpool line of packets.
10079{_}712{_}He was set to singing, and his sweet, clear voice rang out with song after song,-- almost all of them sad ones.
10079{_}713{_}At last one of the crew called on him for a song which he made some demur at singing.
10079{_}714{_}I remember the refrain well (for he _did_ sing it at last); it ran thus:--    "My crew are tried, my bark's my pride, I'm the Pirate of the Isles."
10079{_}715{_}It was no rose-water piracy that the boy sang of; it was the genuine pirate of the Isle of Pines,--the gentleman who before the days of California and steamers was the terror of the Spanish Main.
10079{_}716{_}He was depicted as falling in deadly combat with a naval cruiser, after many desperate deeds.
10079{_}717{_}What was most striking to us of the cabin was, that the sympathy of the song, and evidently of the hearers, was all on the side of the defier of law and order.
10079{_}718{_}There was no nonsense in it about "islands on the face of the deep where the winds never blow and the skies never weep," which to the parlor pirate are the indications of a capital station for wood and water, and for spending his honeymoon.
10079{_}719{_}It was downright cutting of throats and scuttling of ships that our youngster sang of, and the grim faces looked and listened approvingly, as you might fancy Ulysses's veterans hearkening to a tale of Troy.
10079{_}720{_}There is another class of songs, half of the sea, half of the shore, which the fishermen and coasters croon in their lonely watches.
10079{_}721{_}Such is the rhyme of "Uncle Peleg," or "Pillick," as it is pronounced,--probably an historical ballad concerning some departed worthy of the Folger family of Nantucket.
10079{_}722{_}It begins--    "Old Uncle Pillick he built him a boat     On the ba-a-ck side of Nantucket P'int;   He rolled up his trowsers and set her afloat   From the ba-a-ck side of Nantucket P'int."
10079{_}723{_}Like "Christabel," this remains a fragment.
10079{_}724{_}Not so the legend of "Captain Cottington," (or Coddington,) which perhaps is still traditionally known to the young gentlemen at Harvard.
10079{_}725{_}It is marked by a bold and ingenious metrical novelty.
10079{_}726{_}"Captain Cottington he went to sea,   Captain Cottington he went to sea,   Captain Cottington he went to sea-e-e,   Captain Cottington he went to sea."
10079{_}727{_}The third verse of the next stanza announces that he didn't go to sea in a schoo-oo-ooner,--of the next that he went to sea in a bri-i-ig,--and so on.
10079{_}728{_}We learn that he got wrecked on the "Ba-ha-ha-hamys," that he swam ashore with the papers in his hat, and, I believe, entered his protest at the nearest "Counsel's" (_Anglice_.
10079{_}729{_}Consul's) dwelling.
10079{_}730{_}For the amateur of genuine ballad verse, here is a field quite as fertile as that which was reaped by Scott and Ritson amid the border peels and farmhouses of Liddesdale.
10079{_}731{_}It is not unlikely that some treasures may thus be brought to light.
10079{_}732{_}The genuine expression of popular feeling is always forcible, not seldom poetic.
10079{_}733{_}And at any rate, these wild bits of verse are redolent of the freshness of the sea-breeze, the damps of the clinging fog, the strange odors of the caboose-cookery, of the curing of cod, and of many another "ancient and fish-like smell."
10079{_}734{_}Who will tell us of these songs, not indeed of the deep sea, but of soundings?
10079{_}735{_}What were the stanzas which Luckie Mucklebackit sang along the Portanferry Sands?
10079{_}736{_}What is the dredging-song which the oyster "come of a gentle kind" is said to love?
10079{_}737{_}These random thoughts may serve to indicate to the true seeker new and unworked mines of rhythmic ore. We are crying continually, that we have no national literature, that we are a nation of imitators and plagiarists.
10079{_}738{_}Why will not some one take the trouble to learn what we have?
10079{_}739{_}This does not mean that amateurs should endeavor to write such ballad fragments and popular songs,--because that cannot be done; such things grow,--they are not made.
10079{_}740{_}If the sea wants songs, it will have them.
10079{_}741{_}It is only suggested here that we look about us and ascertain of what lyric blessings we may now be the unconscious possessors.
10079{_}742{_}Can it be that oars have risen and fallen, sails flapped, waves broken in thunder upon our shores in vain?
10079{_}743{_}that no whistle of the winds, or moan of the storm-foreboding seas has waked a responsive chord in the heart of pilot or fisherman?
10079{_}744{_}If we are so poor, let us know our poverty.
10079{_}745{_}And now to bring these desultory remarks to a practical conclusion.
10079{_}746{_}I have written these seemingly trifling fragments with a serious purpose.
10079{_}747{_}It is to show that the seaman has little or no art or part in the poetry of the seas.
10079{_}748{_}I have put down facts, have given what experience I have had of some of the idiosyncrasies of the forecastle.
10079{_}749{_}The poetry of the sea has been written on shore and by landsmen.
10079{_}750{_}Falconer's "Shipwreck" is a clever nautical tract, written in verse,--or if it be anything more, it is but the solitary exception which proves and enforces the rule.
10079{_}751{_}Midshipmen have written ambitious verses about the sea; but by the time the young gentlemen were promoted to the ward-room they have dropped the habit or found other themes for their stanzas.
10079{_}752{_}In truth, the stern manliness of his calling forbids the seaman to write poetry.
10079{_}753{_}He acts it.
10079{_}754{_}His is a profession which leaves no room for any assumed feeling or for any reflective tendencies.
10079{_}755{_}His instincts are developed, rather than his reason.
10079{_}756{_}He has no time to speculate.
10079{_}757{_}He must be prepared to lay his hand on the right rope, let the night be the darkest that ever came down upon the waves.
10079{_}758{_}He obeys orders, heedless of consequences; he issues commands amid the uproar and tumult of pressing emergencies.
10079{_}759{_}There is no chance for quackery in his work.
10079{_}760{_}The wind and the wave are infallible tests of all his knots and splices.
10079{_}761{_}He cannot cheat them.
10079{_}762{_}The gale and the lee-shore are not pictures, but fierce realities, with which he has to grapple for life or death.
10079{_}763{_}The soldier and the fireman may pass for heroes upon an assumed stock of courage; but the seaman must be a brave man in his calling, or Nature steps in and brands him coward.
10079{_}764{_}Therefore he cares little about the romance of his duties.
10079{_}765{_}If you would win his interest and regard, it must be on the side of his personal and human sensibilities.
10079{_}766{_}Cut off during his whole active life from any but the most partial sympathy with his kind, he yearns for the life of the shore, its social pleasures and its friendly greetings.
10079{_}767{_}Captains, whose vessels have been made hells-afloat by their tyranny, have found abundant testimony in the courts of law to their gentle and humane deportment on land.
10079{_}768{_}Therefore, when you would address seamen effectively, either in acts or words, let it be by no shallow mimicry of what you fancy to be their life afloat.
10079{_}769{_}It will be at best but "shop" to them, and we all know how distasteful that is in the mouth of a stranger to our pursuits.
10079{_}770{_}They laugh at your clumsy imitations, or are puzzled by your strange misconceptions.
10079{_}771{_}It is painful to see the forlorn attempts which are made to raise the condition of this noble race of men, to read the sad nonsense that is perpetrated for their benefit.
10079{_}772{_}If you wish really to benefit them, it must be by raising their characters as men; and to do this, you must address them as such, irrespectively of the technicalities of their calling.
10079{_}773{_}THE KINLOCH ESTATE, AND HOW IT WAS SETTLED.
10079{_}774{_}CHAPTER I.
10079{_}775{_}"Mildred, my daughter, I am faint.
10079{_}776{_}Run and get me a glass of cordial from the buffet."
10079{_}777{_}The girl looked at her father as he sat in his bamboo chair on the piazza, his pipe just let fall on the floor, and his face covered with a deadly pallor.
10079{_}778{_}She ran for the cordial, and poured it out with a trembling hand.
10079{_}779{_}"Shan't I go for the doctor, father?"
10079{_}780{_}she asked.
10079{_}781{_}"No, my dear, the spasm will pass off presently."
10079{_}782{_}But his face grew more ashy pale, and his jaw drooped.
10079{_}783{_}"Dear father," said the frightened girl, "what shall I do for you?
10079{_}784{_}Oh, dear, if mother were only at home, or Hugh, to run for the doctor!"
10079{_}785{_}"Mildred, my daughter," he gasped with difficulty, "the blacksmith,--send for Ralph Hardwick,--quick!
10079{_}786{_}In the ebony cabinet, middle drawer, you will find----Oh!
10079{_}787{_}oh!--God bless you, my daughter!--God bless"----  The angels, only, heard the conclusion of the sentence; for the speaker, Walter Kinloch, was dead, summoned to the invisible world without a warning and with hardly a struggle.
10079{_}788{_}But Mildred thought he had fainted, and, raising the window, called loudly for Lucy Ransom, the only female domestic then in the house.
10079{_}789{_}Lucy, frightened out of her wits at the sudden call, came rushing to the piazza, flat-iron in hand, and stood riveted to the spot where she first saw the features on which the awful shadow of death had settled.
10079{_}790{_}"Rub his hands, Lucy!"
10079{_}791{_}said Mildred.
10079{_}792{_}"Run for some water!
10079{_}793{_}Get me the smelling-salts!"
10079{_}794{_}Lucy attempted to obey all three orders at once, and therefore did nothing.
10079{_}795{_}Mildred held the unresisting hand.
10079{_}796{_}"It is warm," she said.
10079{_}797{_}"But the pulse,--I can't find it."
10079{_}798{_}"Deary, no," said Lucy, "you won't find it."
10079{_}799{_}"Why, you don't mean"----  "Yes, Mildred, he's dead!"
10079{_}800{_}And she let fall her flat-iron, and covered her face with her apron.
10079{_}801{_}But Mildred kept chafing her father's temples and hands,--calling piteously, in hopes to get an answer from the motionless lips.
10079{_}802{_}Then she sank down at his feet, and clasped his knees in an agony of grief.
10079{_}803{_}A carriage stopped at the door, and a hasty step came up the walk.
10079{_}804{_}"Lucy Ransom," said Mrs. Kinloch, (for it was she, just returned from her drive,) "Lucy Ransom, what are you blubbering about?
10079{_}805{_}Here on the piazza, and with your flat-iron!
10079{_}806{_}What is the matter?"
10079{_}807{_}"Matter enough!"
10079{_}808{_}said Lucy.
10079{_}809{_}"See!--see Mr."----But the sobs were too frequent.
10079{_}810{_}She became choked, and fell into an hysterical paroxysm.
10079{_}811{_}By this time Mrs. Kinloch had stepped upon the piazza, and saw the drooping head, the dangling arms, and the changed face of her husband.
10079{_}812{_}"Dead!
10079{_}813{_}dead!"
10079{_}814{_}she exclaimed.
10079{_}815{_}"My God!
10079{_}816{_}what has happened?
10079{_}817{_}Mildred, who was with him?
10079{_}818{_}Was the doctor sent for?
10079{_}819{_}or Squire Clamp?
10079{_}820{_}or Mr. Rook?
10079{_}821{_}What did he say to you, dear?"
10079{_}822{_}And she tried to lift up the sobbing child, who still clung to the stiffening knees where she had so often climbed for a kiss.
10079{_}823{_}"Oh, mother!
10079{_}824{_}_is_ he dead?--no life left?"
10079{_}825{_}"Calm yourself, my dear child," said Mrs. Kinloch.
10079{_}826{_}"Tell me, did he say anything?"
10079{_}827{_}Mildred replied, "He was faint, and before I could give him the cordial he asked for he was almost gone.
10079{_}828{_}'The blacksmith,' he said, 'send for Ralph Hardwick'; then he said something of the ebony cabinet, but could not speak the words which were on his lips."
10079{_}829{_}She could say no more, but gave way to uncontrollable tears and sobs.
10079{_}830{_}By this time, Mrs. Kinloch's son, Hugh Branning, who had been to the stable with the horse and carriage, came whistling through the yard, and cutting off weeds or twigs along the path with sharp cuts of his whip.
10079{_}831{_}"Which way is the wind now?"
10079{_}832{_}said he, as he approached; "the governor asleep, Mildred crying, and you scolding, mother?"
10079{_}833{_}In a moment, however, the sight of the ghastly face transfixed the thoughtless youth, as it had done his mother; and, dropping his whip, he stood silent, awe-struck, in the presence of the dead.
10079{_}834{_}"Hugh," said Mrs. Kinloch, speaking in a very quiet tone, "go and tell Squire Clamp to come over here."
10079{_}835{_}In a few minutes the dead body was carried into the house by George, the Asiatic servant, aided by a villager who happened to pass by.
10079{_}836{_}Squire Clamp, the lawyer of the town, came and had a conference with Mrs. Kinloch respecting the funeral.
10079{_}837{_}Neighbors came to offer sympathy, and aid, if need should be.
10079{_}838{_}Then the house was put in order, and crape hung on the door- handle.
10079{_}839{_}The family were alone with their dead.
10079{_}840{_}On the village green the boys were playing a grand game of "round ball," for it was a half-holiday.
10079{_}841{_}The clear, silvery tones of the bell were heard, and we stopped to listen.
10079{_}842{_}Was it a fire?
10079{_}843{_}No, the ringing was not vehement enough.
10079{_}844{_}A meeting of the church?
10079{_}845{_}In a moment we should know.
10079{_}846{_}As the bell ceased, we looked up to the white taper spire to catch the next sound.
10079{_}847{_}One stroke.
10079{_}848{_}It was a death, then,--and of a man.
10079{_}849{_}We listened for the age tolled from the belfry.
10079{_}850{_}Fifty-five.
10079{_}851{_}Who had departed?
10079{_}852{_}The sexton crossed the green on his way to the shop to make the coffin, and informed us.
10079{_}853{_}Our bats and balls had lost their interest for us; we did not even ask our tally-man, who cut notches for us on a stick, how the game stood.
10079{_}854{_}For Squire Walter Kinloch was the most considerable man in our village of Innisfield.
10079{_}855{_}Without being highly educated, he was a man of reading and intelligence.
10079{_}856{_}In early life he had amassed a fortune in the China trade, and with it he had brought back a deeply bronzed complexion, a scar from the creese of a Malay pirate, and the easy manners which travel always gives to observant and sensible men.
10079{_}857{_}But his rather stately carriage produced no envy or ill-will among his humbler neighbors, for his superiority was never questioned.
10079{_}858{_}Men bowed to him with honest good-will, and boys, who had been flogged at school for confounding Congo and Coromandel, and putting Borneo in the Bight of Benin, made an awkward obeisance and stared wonderingly, as they met the man who had actually sailed round the world, and had, in his own person, illustrated the experiment of walking with his head downwards among the antipodes.
10079{_}859{_}His house had no rival in the country round, and his garden was considered a miracle of art, having, in popular belief, all the fruits, flowers, and shrubs that had been known from the days of Solomon to those of Linnaeus.
10079{_}860{_}Prodigious stories were told of his hoard of gold, and some of the less enlightened thought that even the outlandish ornaments of the balustrade over the portico were carven silver.
10079{_}861{_}Curious vases adorned the hall and side-board; and numberless quaint trinkets, whose use the villagers could not even imagine, gave to the richly-furnished rooms an air of Oriental magnificence.
10079{_}862{_}Tropical birds sang or chattered in cages, and a learned but lawless parrot talked, swore, or made mischief, as he chose.
10079{_}863{_}The tawny servant George, brought by Mr. Kinloch from one of the islands of the Pacific, completed his claims upon the admiration of the untravelled.
10079{_}864{_}He was just ready to enjoy the evening of life, when the night of death closed upon him with tropic suddenness.
10079{_}865{_}He left one child only, his daughter Mildred, then just turned of eighteen; and as Mrs. Kinloch had only one son to claim her affection, the motherless girl would seem to be well provided for.
10079{_}866{_}Mildred was sweet-tempered, and her step-mother had hitherto been discreet and kind.
10079{_}867{_}The funeral was over, and the townspeople recovered from the shock which the sudden death had caused.
10079{_}868{_}Administration was granted to the widow conjointly with Squire Clamp, the lawyer, and the latter was appointed guardian for Mildred during her minority.
10079{_}869{_}Squire Clamp was an ill-favored man, heavy-browed and bald, and with a look which, in a person of less consequence, would have been called "hang- dog,"--owing partly, no doubt, to the tribulation he had suffered from his vixen spouse, whose tongue was now happily silenced.
10079{_}870{_}He was the town's only lawyer, (a fortunate circumstance,) so that he could frequently manage to receive fees for advice from both parties in a controversy.
10079{_}871{_}He made all the wills, deeds, and contracts, and settled all the estates he could get hold of.
10079{_}872{_}But no such prize as the Kinloch property had ever before come into his hands.
10079{_}873{_}If Squire Clamp's reputation for shrewdness had belonged to an irreligious man, it would have been of questionable character; but as he was a zealous member of the church, he was protected from assaults upon his integrity.
10079{_}874{_}If there were suspicions, they were kept close, not bruited abroad.
10079{_}875{_}He was now an almost daily visitor at the widow Kinloch's.
10079{_}876{_}What was the intricate business that required the constant attention of a legal adviser?
10079{_}877{_}The settlement of the estate, so far as the world knew, was an easy matter.
10079{_}878{_}The property consisted of the dwelling-house, a small tract of land near the village, a manufactory at the dam, by the side of Ralph Hardwick's blacksmith's shop, and money, plate, furniture, and stocks.
10079{_}879{_}There were no debts.
10079{_}880{_}There was but one child, and, after the assignment of the widow's dower, the estate was Mildred's.
10079{_}881{_}Nothing, therefore, could be simpler for the administrators.
10079{_}882{_}The girl trusted to the good faith of her stepmother and the justice of the lawyer, who now stood to her in the place of a father.
10079{_}883{_}She was an orphan, and her innocence and childlike dependence would doubtless be a sufficient spur to the consciences of her protectors.
10079{_}884{_}So the girl thought, if she thought at all,--and so all charitable people were bound to think.
10079{_}885{_}How wearily the days passed during the month after the funeral!
10079{_}886{_}The shadow of death seemed to darken everything.
10079{_}887{_}Doors creaked dismally when they were opened.
10079{_}888{_}The room where the body had been laid seemed to have grown a century older than the other parts of the once bright and cheerful house, --its atmosphere was so stagnant and full of mould.
10079{_}889{_}The family spoke only in suppressed tones; their countenances were as sad as their garments.
10079{_}890{_}All this was terrible to the impressible, imaginative, and naturally buoyant temper of Mildred.
10079{_}891{_}It was like dwelling in a tomb, and her heart cried out for very loneliness.
10079{_}892{_}She must do something to take her mind out of the sunless vault,--she must resume her relations with the dwellers in the upper air.
10079{_}893{_}All at once she thought of her father's last words,--of Ralph Hardwick, and the ebony cabinet.
10079{_}894{_}It was in the next room.
10079{_}895{_}She opened the door, half expecting to see some bodiless presence in the silent space.
10079{_}896{_}She could hear her own heart beat between the tickings of the great Dutch clock, as she stepped across the floor.
10079{_}897{_}How still was everything!
10079{_}898{_}The air tingled in her ears as though now disturbed for the first time.
10079{_}899{_}She opened the cabinet, which was not locked, and pulled out the middle drawer.
10079{_}900{_}She found nothing but a dried rose-bud and a lock of sunny hair wrapped in a piece of yellowed paper.
10079{_}901{_}Was it her mother's hair?
10079{_}902{_}As Mildred remembered her mother, the color of her hair was dark, not golden.
10079{_}903{_}Still it might have been cut in youth, before its hue had deepened.
10079{_}904{_}And what a world of mystery, of feeling, of associations there was in that scentless and withered rose-bud!
10079{_}905{_}What fair hand had first plucked it?
10079{_}906{_}What pledge did it carry?
10079{_}907{_}Was the subtile aroma of love ever blended with its fragrance?
10079{_}908{_}Had her father borne it with him in his wanderings?
10079{_}909{_}The secret was in his coffin.
10079{_}910{_}The struggling lips could not utter it before they were stiffened into marble.
10079{_}911{_}Yet she could not believe that these relics were the sole things to which he had referred.
10079{_}912{_}There must have been something that more nearly concerned her,--something in which the blacksmith or his nephew was interested.
10079{_}913{_}CHAPTER II.
10079{_}914{_}In order to show the position of Mrs. Kinloch and her son in our story, it will be necessary to make the reader acquainted with some previous occurrences.
10079{_}915{_}Six years before this date, Mrs. Kinloch was the Widow Branning.
10079{_}916{_}Her husband's small estate had melted like a snow-bank in the liquidation of his debts.
10079{_}917{_}She had only one child, Hugh, to support; but in a country town there is generally little that a woman can do to earn a livelihood; and she might often have suffered from want, if the neighbors had not relieved her.
10079{_}918{_}If she left her house for any errand, (locks were but seldom used in Innisfield,) she would often on her return find a leg of mutton, a basket of apples or potatoes, or a sack of flour, conveyed there by some unknown hands.
10079{_}919{_}In winter nights she would hear the voices of Ralph Hardwick, the village blacksmith, and his boys, as they drew sled-loads of wood, ready cut and split, to keep up her kitchen fire.
10079{_}920{_}Other friends ploughed and planted her garden, and performed numberless kind offices.
10079{_}921{_}But, though aided in this way by charity, Mrs. Branning never lost her self-respect nor her standing in the neighborhood.
10079{_}922{_}Everybody knew that she was poor, and she knew that everybody knew it; yet so long as she was not in absolute want, and the poor-house, that bugbear of honest poverty, was yet far distant, she managed to keep a cheerful heart, and visited her neighbors on terms of entire equality.
10079{_}923{_}At this period Walter Kinloch's wife died, leaving an only child.
10079{_}924{_}During her sickness, Mrs. Branning had been sent for to act as nurse and temporary house-keeper, and, at the urgent request of the widower, remained for a time after the funeral.
10079{_}925{_}Weeks passed, and her house was still tenantless.
10079{_}926{_}Mildred had become so much attached to the motherly widow and her son, that she would not allow the servants to do anything for her.
10079{_}927{_}So, without any definite agreement, their relations continued.
10079{_}928{_}By-and-by the village gossips began to query and surmise.
10079{_}929{_}At the sewing- society the matter was fully discussed.
10079{_}930{_}Mrs. Greenfield, the doctor's wife, admitted that it would be an excellent match, "jest a child apiece, both on 'em well brought up, used to good company, and all that; but, land's sakes!
10079{_}931{_}he, with his mint o' money, a'n't a-goin' to marry a poor widder that ha'n't got nothin' but her husband's pictur' and her boy,--not he!"
10079{_}932{_}Others insinuated that Mrs. Branning knew what she was about when she went to Squire Kinloch's, and his wife was 'most gone with consumption.
10079{_}933{_}"'Twasn't a mite strange that little Mildred took to her so kindly; plenty of women could find ways to please a child, if so be they could have such a chance to please themselves."
10079{_}934{_}The general opinion seemed to be that Mrs. Branning would marry the Squire, if she could get him; but that as to his intentions, the matter was quite doubtful.
10079{_}935{_}Nevertheless, after being talked about for a year, the parties were duly published, married, and settled down into the quiet routine of country life.
10079{_}936{_}Doubtless the accident of daily contact was the secret of the match.
10079{_}937{_}Had Mrs. Branning been living in her own poorly-furnished house, Mr. Kinloch would hardly have thought of going to seek her.
10079{_}938{_}But as mistress of his establishment she had an opportunity to display her house-wifely qualities, as well as to practise those nameless arts by which almost any clever woman knows how to render herself agreeable.
10079{_}939{_}The first favorable impression deepened, until the widower came to believe that the whole parish did not contain so proper a person to be the successor of Mrs. Kinloch, as his housekeeper.
10079{_}940{_}Their union, though childless, was as happy as common; there was nothing of the romance of a first attachment,--little of the tenderness that springs from fresh sensibilities, for she at least was of a matter-of-fact turn.
10079{_}941{_}But there was a constant and hearty good feeling, resulting from mutual kindness and deference.
10079{_}942{_}If the step-mother made any difference in her treatment of the two children, it was in favor of the gentle Mildred.
10079{_}943{_}And though the Squire naturally felt more affection for his motherless daughter, yet he was proud of his step-son, gave him the advantages of the best schools, and afterwards sent him for a year to college.
10079{_}944{_}But the lad's spirits were too buoyant for the sober notions of the Faculty.
10079{_}945{_}He was king in the gymnasium, and was minutely learned in the natural history and botany of the neighborhood; at least, he knew all the haunts of birds, rabbits, and squirrels, as well as the choicest orchards of fruit.
10079{_}946{_}After repeated admonitions without effect, a letter was addressed to his stepfather by vote at a Faculty-meeting.
10079{_}947{_}A damsel at service in the President's house overheard the discussion, and found means to warn the young delinquent of his danger; for she, as well as most people who came within the sphere of his attraction, felt kindly toward him.
10079{_}948{_}The stage-coach that conveyed the next morning's mail to Innisfield carried Hugh Branning as a passenger.
10079{_}949{_}Alighting at the post-office, he took out the letter superscribed in the well-known hand of the President, pocketed it, and returned by the next stage to college.
10079{_}950{_}This prank only moved the Squire to mirth, when he heard of it.
10079{_}951{_}He knew that Hugh was a lad of spirit,--that in scholarship he was by no means a dunce; and as long as there was no positive tendency to vice, he thought but lightly of his boyish peccadilloes.
10079{_}952{_}But it was impossible for such irregularities to continue, and after a while Mr. Kinloch yielded to his step-son's request and took him home.
10079{_}953{_}Next year it was thought best that the young man should go to sea, and a midshipman's commission was procured for him.
10079{_}954{_}Now, for the second time, after an absence of three years, Hugh was at home in all the dignity of navy blue, anchor buttons, glazed cap, and sword.
10079{_}955{_}CHAPTER III.
10079{_}956{_}"I have brought you the statement of the property, Mrs. Kinloch," said Mr. Clamp.
10079{_}957{_}"It is merely a legal form, embracing the items which you gave to me; it must be returned at the next Probate term."
10079{_}958{_}Mrs. Kinloch took the paper and glanced over it.
10079{_}959{_}"This statement must be sworn to, Mrs.
10079{_}960{_}Kinloch."
10079{_}961{_}"By you?"
10079{_}962{_}"We are joined in the administration, and both must swear to it."
10079{_}963{_}There was a pause.
10079{_}964{_}Mrs. Kinloch, resting her hands on her knee, tossed the hem of her dress with her foot, as though meditating.
10079{_}965{_}"I shall of course readily make oath to the schedule," he continued,--"at least, after you have done so; for I have no personal knowledge of the effects of the deceased."
10079{_}966{_}His manner was decorous, but he regarded her keenly.
10079{_}967{_}She changed the subject.
10079{_}968{_}"People seem to think I have a mint in the house; and _such_ bills as come in!
10079{_}969{_}Sawin, the cabinet-maker, has sent his to-day, as soon as my husband is fairly under ground: forty dollars for a cherry coffin, which he made in one day.
10079{_}970{_}Cleaver, the butcher, too, has sent a bill running back for five years or more.
10079{_}971{_}Now I _know_ that Mr. Kinloch never had an ounce of meat from him that he didn't pay for.
10079{_}972{_}If they all go on in this way, I sha'n't have a cent left.
10079{_}973{_}Everybody tries to cheat the widow"----  "And orphan," interposed Mr. Clamp.
10079{_}974{_}She looked at him quietly; but he was imperturbable.
10079{_}975{_}"We must begin to collect what is due," she continued.
10079{_}976{_}"Did you refer to the notes from Ploughman?"
10079{_}977{_}asked Mr. Clamp.
10079{_}978{_}"He is perfectly good; and he will pay the interest till we want to use the money."
10079{_}979{_}"I wasn't thinking of Ploughman," she replied, "but of Mark Davenport, Uncle Ralph Hardwick's nephew.
10079{_}980{_}They say he is a teacher in one of the fashionable schools in New York,--and he must be able to pay, if he's ever going to."
10079{_}981{_}"Well, when he comes on here, I will present the notes."
10079{_}982{_}"But I don't intend to wait till he comes; can't you send the demands to a lawyer where he is?"
10079{_}983{_}"Certainly, if you wish it; but that course will necessarily be attended with some expense."
10079{_}984{_}"I choose to have it done," said Mrs. Kinloch, decisively.
10079{_}985{_}"Mildred, who has always been foolishly partial to the young upstart, insists that her father intended to give up the notes to Mark, and she thinks that was what he wanted to send for Uncle Ralph about, just before he died.
10079{_}986{_}I don't believe it, and I don't intend to fling away _my_ money upon such folks."
10079{_}987{_}"You are quite right, ma'am," said the lawyer.
10079{_}988{_}"The inconsiderate generosity of school-children would be a poor basis for the transactions of business."
10079{_}989{_}"And besides," continued Mrs. Kinloch, "I want the young man to remember the blacksmith's shop that he came from, and get over his ridiculous notion of looking up to our family."
10079{_}990{_}"Oh ho!"
10079{_}991{_}said Mr. Clamp, "that is it?
10079{_}992{_}Well, you are a sagacious woman,"-- looking at her with unfeigned admiration.
10079{_}993{_}"I _can_ see through a millstone, when there is a hole in it," said Mrs. Kinloch.
10079{_}994{_}"And I mean to stop this nonsense."
10079{_}995{_}"To be sure,--it would be a very unequal match in every way.
10079{_}996{_}Besides, I'm told that he isn't well-grounded in doctrine.
10079{_}997{_}He even goes to Brooklyn to hear Torchlight preach."
10079{_}998{_}And Mr. Clamp rolled up his eyes, interlocking his fingers, as he was wont when at church-meeting he rose to exhort.
10079{_}999{_}"I don't pretend to be a judge of doctrine, further than the catechism goes," said the widow; "but Mr. Rook says that Torchlight is a dangerous man, and will lead the churches off into infidelity."
10079{_}1000{_}"Yes, Mrs. Kinloch, the free-thinking of this age is the fruitful parent of all evil,--of Mormonism, Unitarianism, Spiritualism, and of all those forms of error which seek to overthrow"----  There was a crash in the china-closet.
10079{_}1001{_}Mrs. Kinloch went to the door, and leading out Lucy Ransom, the maid, by the ear, exclaimed, "You hussy, what were you there for?
10079{_}1002{_}I'll teach you to be listening about in closets," (giving the ear a fresh tweak,) "you eavesdropper!"
10079{_}1003{_}"Quit!"
10079{_}1004{_}cried Lucy.
10079{_}1005{_}"I didn't mean to listen.
10079{_}1006{_}I was there rubbin' the silver 'fore you come.
10079{_}1007{_}Then I didn't wanter come out, for I was afeard."
10079{_}1008{_}"What made the smash, then?"
10079{_}1009{_}demanded Mrs. Kinloch.
10079{_}1010{_}"I was settin' things on the top shelf, and the chair tipped over."
10079{_}1011{_}"Don't make it worse by fibbing!
10079{_}1012{_}If that was so, how came the chair to tip the way it did?
10079{_}1013{_}You were trying to peep over the door.
10079{_}1014{_}Go to the kitchen!"
10079{_}1015{_}Lucy went out with fallen plumes.
10079{_}1016{_}Mr. Clamp took his hat to go also.
10079{_}1017{_}"Don't go till I get you the notes," said Mrs. Kinloch.
10079{_}1018{_}As she brought them, he said, "I will send these by the next mail, with instructions to collect."
10079{_}1019{_}While his hand was on the latch, she spoke again:--  "Mr. Clamp, did you ever look over the deed of the land we own about the dam where the mill stands?"
10079{_}1020{_}"No, ma'am, I have never seen it."
10079{_}1021{_}"I wish you would have the land surveyed according to this title," she said.
10079{_}1022{_}"Quite privately, you know.
10079{_}1023{_}Just have the line run, and let me know about it.
10079{_}1024{_}Perhaps it will be as well to send over to Riverbank and get Gunter to do it; he will keep quiet about it."
10079{_}1025{_}Mr. Clamp stood still a moment.
10079{_}1026{_}Here was a woman whom he was expecting to lead like a child, but who on the other hand had fairly bridled and saddled _him_, so that he was driven he knew not whither.
10079{_}1027{_}"Why do you propose this, may I ask, Mrs.
10079{_}1028{_}Kinloch?"
10079{_}1029{_}"Oh, I have heard," she replied, carelessly, "that there was some error in the surveys.
10079{_}1030{_}Mr. Kinloch often talked of having it corrected, but, like most men, put it off.
10079{_}1031{_}Now, as we may sell the property, we shall want to know what we have got."
10079{_}1032{_}"Certainly, Mrs. Kinloch, I will follow your prudent suggestions,"--adding to himself, as he walked away, "I shall have to be tolerably shrewd to get ahead of that woman.
10079{_}1033{_}I wonder what she is driving at."
10079{_}1034{_}CHAPTER IV.
10079{_}1035{_}Ralph Hardwick was the village blacksmith.
10079{_}1036{_}His shop stood on the bank of the river, not far from the dam.
10079{_}1037{_}The great wheel below the flume rolled all day, throwing over its burden of diamond drops, and tilting the ponderous hammer with a monotonous clatter.
10079{_}1038{_}What a palace of wonders to the boys was that grim and sooty shop!--the roar of the fires, as they were fed by the laboring bellows; the sound of water, rushing, gurgling, or musically dropping, heard in the pauses; the fiery shower of sparkles that flew when the trip-hammer fell; and the soft and glowing mass held by the smith's tongs with firm grasp, and turning to some form of use under his practised eye!
10079{_}1039{_}How proud were the young amateur blacksmiths when the kind-hearted owner of the shop gave them liberty to heat and pound a bit of nail-rod, to mend a skate or a sled-runner, or sharpen a pronged fish- spear!
10079{_}1040{_}Still happier were they, when, at night, with his sons and nephew, they were allowed to huddle on the forge, sitting on the bottoms of old buckets or boxes, and watching the fire, from the paly blue border of flame in the edge of the damp charcoal, to the reddening, glowing column that shot with an arrowy stream of sparks up the wide-throated chimney.
10079{_}1041{_}How the dark rafters and nail-pierced roof grew ruddy as the white-hot ploughshare or iron bar was drawn from the fire!--what alternations of light and shadow!
10079{_}1042{_}No painter ever drew figure in such relief as the blacksmith presented in that wonderful light, with his glistening face, his tense muscles, and his upraised arm.
10079{_}1043{_}Alas!
10079{_}1044{_}the hammer is still; the wheel dashes no more the glittering spray; the fire has died out in the forge; the blacksmith's long day's work is done!
10079{_}1045{_}He settled in Innisfield when it was but a district attached to a neighboring town.
10079{_}1046{_}There were but three or four houses in the now somewhat populous village.
10079{_}1047{_}He came on foot, driving his cow; his wife following in the wagon, with their little stock of household goods,--not forgetting his hammer, more potent than Prospero's wand.
10079{_}1048{_}The minister, the doctor, and Squire Kinloch, who constituted the aristocracy, yielded precedence in date to Ralph Hardwick, Knight of the Ancient Order of the Anvil.
10079{_}1049{_}So he toiled, faithful to his calling.
10079{_}1050{_}By day the din of his hammer rarely ceased, and by night the flame and sparks from his chimney were a Pharos to all travellers approaching the town.
10079{_}1051{_}Children were born to him, for which he blessed God, and worked the harder.
10079{_}1052{_}He attained a moderate prosperity, secure from want, but still dependent upon labor for bread.
10079{_}1053{_}At length his wife died; he wept like a true and faithful husband as he was, and thenceforth was both mother and father to his babes.
10079{_}1054{_}During all his life he kept Sunday with religious scrupulousness, and with his family went to the house of worship in all weathers.
10079{_}1055{_}From the very first he had been leader of the choir, and had given the pitch with a fork hammered and tuned by his own hands.
10079{_}1056{_}With a clear and sympathetic voice, he had such an instinctive taste and power of expression, that his song of penitence or praise was far more devotional than the labored efforts of many more highly cultivated singers.
10079{_}1057{_}Music and poetry flowed smoothly and naturally from his lips, but in uttering the common prose of daily life his organs were rebellious.
10079{_}1058{_}The truth must be spoken,--he stammered badly, incurably.
10079{_}1059{_}Whether it was owing to the attempt to overcome his impediment by making his speech musical, or to the cadences of his hammer beating time while his brain was shaping its airy fancies, his thoughts ran naturally in verse.
10079{_}1060{_}Do not smile at the thought of Vulcan's callused fingers touching the chords of the lyre to delicate music.
10079{_}1061{_}The sun shone as lovingly upon the swart face of the blacksmith in his shop-door, as upon the scholar at his library-window.
10079{_}1062{_}"Poetry was an angel in his breast," making his heart glad with her heavenly presence; he did not "make her his drudge, his maid-of- all-work," as professional verse-makers do.
10079{_}1063{_}Mr. Hardwick's younger sister was married to a hard-working, stern, puritanical man named Davenport, (not her first love,) who removed to a Western State when it was almost a wilderness, cleared for himself a farm, and built a log-house.
10079{_}1064{_}The toil and privations of frontier life soon wrought their natural effects upon Mrs. Davenport's delicate constitution.
10079{_}1065{_}She fell into a rapid decline and died.
10079{_}1066{_}Her husband was seized with a fever the summer after, and died also, leaving two children, Mark and Anna.
10079{_}1067{_}The blacksmith had six motherless children of his own; but he set out for the West, and brought the orphans home with him.
10079{_}1068{_}He thenceforth treated them like his own offspring, manifesting a woman's tenderness as well as a father's care for them.
10079{_}1069{_}Mark was a comely lad, with the yellow curling hair, the clear blue eyes, and the marked symmetry of features that belonged to his uncle.
10079{_}1070{_}He had an inborn love of reading and study; he was first in his class at every winter's school, and had devoured all the books within his reach.
10079{_}1071{_}Then he borrowed an old copy of Adam's Latin Grammar from Dr. Greenfield, and committed the rules to memory without a teacher.
10079{_}1072{_}That was his introduction to the classics.
10079{_}1073{_}But Mr. Hardwick believed in the duty and excellence of work, and Mark, as well as his cousins, was trained to make himself useful.
10079{_}1074{_}So the Grammar was studied and Virgil read at chance intervals, when a storm interrupted out-door work, or while waiting at the upper mill for a grist, or of nights at the shop by the light of the forge fire.
10079{_}1075{_}The paradigms were committed to memory with an anvil accompaniment; and long after, he never could scan a line of Homer, especially the oft-repeated  [Greek: Tou d'au | Taelema | chos pep | numenos | antion | aeuda],  without hearing the ringing blows of his uncle's hammer keeping tune to the verse.
10079{_}1076{_}At sixteen years of age he was ready to enter college, though he had received little aid in his studies, except when some schoolmaster who was versed in the humanities chanced to be hired for the winter.
10079{_}1077{_}But his uncle was not able to support him at any respectable university, and the lad's prospects for such an education as he desired seemed to be none of the best.
10079{_}1078{_}At this point an incident occurred which changed the course of our hero's life, and as it will serve to explain how he came to give his notes to Mr. Kinloch, on which the administrators are about to bring suit, it should properly be related here.
10079{_}1079{_}Mark Davenport was at work on a farm a short distance from the village.
10079{_}1080{_}He hoped to enter college the following autumn, and he knew no means to obtain money for a portion of his outfit except by the labor of his hands.
10079{_}1081{_}He could get twenty dollars a month for the summer season.
10079{_}1082{_}Sixty, or possibly seventy dollars!--what ideas of opulence were suggested by the sound of those words!
10079{_}1083{_}It was a damp, drizzly day; there was not a settled rain, yet it was too wet to work in the corn.
10079{_}1084{_}Mark was therefore busy in picking loose stones from the surface of a field cultivated the year before, and now "seeded down" for grass.
10079{_}1085{_}A portion of the field bordered on a pond, and the alders upon its margin formed a dense green palisade, over which might be seen the gray surface of the water freckled by the tiny drops of rain.
10079{_}1086{_}Low clouds trailed their gauzy robes over the top of Mount Quobbin, and flecks of mist swept across the blue sides of the loftier Mount Elizabeth.
10079{_}1087{_}"What a perfect day for fishing!"
10079{_}1088{_}thought Mark.
10079{_}1089{_}"If I had my tackle here, and a frog's leg or a shiner, I would soon have a pickerel out from under those lilypads."
10079{_}1090{_}But he kept at work, and, having his basket full of stones, carried them to the pond and plumped them in.
10079{_}1091{_}A growl of anger came up from behind the bushes.
10079{_}1092{_}"What the Devil do you mean, you lubber, throwing stones over here to scare away the fish?"
10079{_}1093{_}The bushes parted at the same time, showing Hugh Branning sitting in the end of his boat, and apparently just ready to fling out his line.
10079{_}1094{_}"If I had known you were there fishing," said Mark, "I shouldn't have thrown the stones into the water.
10079{_}1095{_}But," he continued, while every fibre tingled with indignation, "I will have you to know that I am not to be talked to in that way by you or anybody else."
10079{_}1096{_}"I would like to know how you are going to help yourself," said Hugh, stepping ashore and advancing.
10079{_}1097{_}"You will find out, Mr. Insolence, if you don't leave this field.
10079{_}1098{_}You a'n't on the quarter-deck yet, bullying a tar with his hat off."
10079{_}1099{_}"Bless me!
10079{_}1100{_}how the young Vulcan talks!"
10079{_}1101{_}"I have talked all I am going to.
10079{_}1102{_}Now get into your boat and be off!"
10079{_}1103{_}"I don't propose to be in a hurry," said Hugh, with provoking coolness, standing with his arms a-kimbo.
10079{_}1104{_}The remembrance of Hugh's usual patronizing airs, together with his insulting language, was too much for Mark's impetuous temper.
10079{_}1105{_}He was in a delirium of rage, and he rushed upon his antagonist.
10079{_}1106{_}Hugh stood warily upon the defensive, and parried Mark's blows with admirable skill; he had not the muscle nor the endurance of the young blacksmith, but he had considerable skill in boxing, and was perfectly cool; and though Mark finally succeeded in grappling and hurling to the ground his lithe and resolute foe, it was not until he had been pretty severely pommelled himself, especially in his face.
10079{_}1107{_}Mark set his knee on the breast of his adversary and waited to hear "Enough."
10079{_}1108{_}Hugh ground his teeth, but there was no escape; no feint nor sudden movement could reverse their positions; and, out of breath, he gave up in sullen despair.
10079{_}1109{_}"Let me up," he said, at length.
10079{_}1110{_}Mark arose, and being by this time thoroughly sobered, he walked off without a word and picked up his basket.
10079{_}1111{_}Hugh, on the other hand, was more and more angry every minute.
10079{_}1112{_}The indignity he had suffered was not to be tamely submitted to.
10079{_}1113{_}He got into the boat and took his oar; he looked back and saw Mark commencing work again; the temptation was too strong.
10079{_}1114{_}He picked up one of the largest of the stones that Mark had emptied into the shallow margin of the pond; he threw it with all his force, and hurriedly pushed off from shore without stopping to ascertain the extent of the mischief he had done.
10079{_}1115{_}He knew that the stone did not miss, for he saw Mark fall heavily to the ground, and that was enough.
10079{_}1116{_}The injury was serious.
10079{_}1117{_}Mark was carried to the farm- house and was confined to his bed for six weeks with a brain fever, being delirious for the greater part of the time.
10079{_}1118{_}Hugh Branning found the town quite uncomfortable; the eyes of all the people he met seemed to scorch him.
10079{_}1119{_}He was bold and self-reliant; but no man can stand up singly against the indignation of a whole community.
10079{_}1120{_}He went on a visit to Boston, and not long after, to the exceeding grief of his mother, entered the navy.
10079{_}1121{_}When Mark was recovering, Mr. Rook, the clergyman, called and offered to aid him in his college course, if he would agree to study for the ministry.
10079{_}1122{_}But the young man declined the proposal, because he thought himself unfitted for the sacred calling.
10079{_}1123{_}"No," he added, with a smile, "I'm not made for an evangelist; not much like the beloved disciple at all events, but rather like peppery Peter,-- ready, if provoked, to whisk off an ignoble ear."
10079{_}1124{_}Mr. Rook returned home sorrowful; and at the next meeting of the sewing- circle the unfortunate Mark received a full share of attention; for the offer of aid came partly from this society.
10079{_}1125{_}When this matter had been the talk of the village for a day or two, Squire Kinloch made some errand to the house where Mark was.
10079{_}1126{_}What passed between them the young man did not choose to relate, but he showed his Uncle Hardwick the Squire's check for two hundred and fifty dollars, and told him he should receive a similar sum each year until he finished his collegiate course.
10079{_}1127{_}The promise was kept; the yearly supply was furnished; and Mark graduated with honor, having given notes amounting to a thousand dollars.
10079{_}1128{_}With cheerful alacrity he commenced teaching in a popular seminary, intending to pay his debts before studying a profession.
10079{_}1129{_}CHAPTER V.  It was Saturday night, and Mr. Hardwick was closing his shop.
10079{_}1130{_}A customer was just leaving, his horse's feet newly rasped and white, and a sack of harrow-teeth thrown across his back.
10079{_}1131{_}The boys, James and Milton, had been putting a load of charcoal under cover, for the wind was southerly and there were signs of rain.
10079{_}1132{_}Of course they had become black enough with coal-dust,--not a streak of light was visible, except around their eyes.
10079{_}1133{_}They were capering about and contemplating each other's face with uproarious delight, while the blacksmith, though internally chuckling at their antics, preserved a decent gravity, and prepared to go to his house.
10079{_}1134{_}He drew a bucket of water, and bared his muscular arms, then, after washing them, soused his curly hair and begrimed face, and came out wonderfully brightened by the operation.
10079{_}1135{_}The boys continued their sports, racing, wrestling, and putting on grotesque grimaces.
10079{_}1136{_}Charlotte, the youngest child, now came to the shop to say that supper was ready.
10079{_}1137{_}"C-come, boys, you've ha-had play enough," said Mr. Hardwick.
10079{_}1138{_}"J-James, put Ch-Charlotte down.
10079{_}1139{_}M-M-Milton, it's close on to S-Sabba'day.
10079{_}1140{_}Now w- wash yourselves."
10079{_}1141{_}Just as the merriment was highest, Charlotte standing on James's shoulders, and Milton chasing them, while the blacksmith was looking on,-- his honest face glistening with soap and good-humor,--Mildred Kinloch passed by on her way home from a walk by the river.
10079{_}1142{_}She looked towards the shop-door and bowed to Mr. Hardwick.
10079{_}1143{_}"G-good evenin', M-Miss Mildred," said he; "I'm g-glad to see you lookin' so ch-cheerful."
10079{_}1144{_}The tone was hearty, and with a dash of chivalrous sentiment rarely heard in a smithy.
10079{_}1145{_}His look of half-parental, half-admiring fondness was touching to see.
10079{_}1146{_}"Oh, Uncle Ralph," she replied, "I am never melancholy when I see you.
10079{_}1147{_}You have all the cheerfulness of this spring day in your face."
10079{_}1148{_}"Y-yes, I hev to stay here in the old shop; b-but I hear the b-birds in the mornin', and all day I f-feel as ef I was out under the b-blue sky, an' rejoicin' with all livin' creaturs in the sun and the s-sweet air of heaven."
10079{_}1149{_}"I envy you your happy frame; everything has some form or hue of beauty for you.
10079{_}1150{_}I must have you read to me again.
10079{_}1151{_}I never take up Milton without thinking of you."
10079{_}1152{_}"I c-couldn't wish to be remembered in any p-pleasanter way."
10079{_}1153{_}"Well, good evening.
10079{_}1154{_}I must hurry home, for it grows damp here by the  mill-race.
10079{_}1155{_}Tell Lizzy and Anna to come and see me.
10079{_}1156{_}We are quite lonesome now."
10079{_}1157{_}"P-p'raps Mark'll come with 'em."
10079{_}1158{_}"Mark?
10079{_}1159{_}Is he here?
10079{_}1160{_}When did he come?"
10079{_}1161{_}"H-he'll be here t-to-night."
10079{_}1162{_}"You surprise me!"
10079{_}1163{_}"'Tis rather s-sudden.
10079{_}1164{_}He wrote y-yes-terday 't he'd g-got to come on urgent b-business."
10079{_}1165{_}"Urgent business?"
10079{_}1166{_}she repeated, thoughtfully.
10079{_}1167{_}"I wonder if Squire Clamp"----  The blacksmith nodded, with a gesture towards his children, as though he would not have them hear.
10079{_}1168{_}"Yes," he added, in a low tone, "I g-guess that is it."
10079{_}1169{_}"I must go home," said Mildred, hurriedly.
10079{_}1170{_}"Well, G-God bless you, my daughter!
10079{_}1171{_}D-don't forgit your old sooty friend.
10079{_}1172{_}And ef ever y-you want the help of a s-stout hand, or of an old gray head, don't fail to come to the ber-blacksmith's shop."
10079{_}1173{_}"Thank you, Uncle Ralph!
10079{_}1174{_}thank you with all my heart!
10079{_}1175{_}Good-night!"
10079{_}1176{_}She walked lightly up the hill towards the principal street.
10079{_}1177{_}But she had not gone half a dozen yards before a hand grasped her arm.
10079{_}1178{_}She turned with a start.
10079{_}1179{_}"Mark Davenport!"
10079{_}1180{_}she exclaimed, "Is it you?
10079{_}1181{_}How you frightened me!"
10079{_}1182{_}"Yes, Mildred, it is Mark, your old friend" (with a meaning emphasis).
10079{_}1183{_}"I couldn't resist the temptation of giving you a little surprise."
10079{_}1184{_}"But when did you come to town?"
10079{_}1185{_}"I have just reached here from the station at Riverbank.
10079{_}1186{_}I went to the house first, and was just going to see Uncle at the shop, when I caught sight of you."
10079{_}1187{_}Mark drew her arm within his own, and noticed, not without pleasure, how she yet trembled with agitation.
10079{_}1188{_}"I am very glad to see you," said Mildred; "but isn't your coming sudden?"
10079{_}1189{_}"Yes, I had some news from home yesterday which determined me to come, and I started this morning."
10079{_}1190{_}"Quick and impetuous as ever!"
10079{_}1191{_}"Yes, I don't deliberate long."
10079{_}1192{_}There was a pause.
10079{_}1193{_}"I wish you had only been here to see father before he died."
10079{_}1194{_}"I wish I might have seen him."
10079{_}1195{_}"I am sure _he_ would never have desired to put you to any trouble."
10079{_}1196{_}"I suppose he would not have _troubled_ me, though I never expected to do less than repay him the money he was so good as to lend me; but I don't think he would have been so abrupt and peremptory as Squire Clamp."
10079{_}1197{_}"Why, what has he done?"
10079{_}1198{_}"This is what he has done.
10079{_}1199{_}A lawyer's clerk, as I supposed him to be, called upon me yesterday morning with a statement of the debt and interest, and made a formal demand of payment.
10079{_}1200{_}I had only about half the amount in bank, and therefore could not meet it.
10079{_}1201{_}Then the clerk appeared in his true character as a sheriff's officer, drew out his papers, and served a writ upon me, besides a trustee process on the principal of the school, so as to attach whatever might be due to me."
10079{_}1202{_}"Oh, Mark, were you treated so?"
10079{_}1203{_}"Just so,--entrapped like a wild animal.
10079{_}1204{_}To be sure, it was a legal process, but one designed only for extreme cases, and which no gentleman ever puts in force against another."
